10077{_}572{_}He shrewdly suspected Palmer wasn't "sound."
10077{_}573{_}No patriot would go into the war with such a miserable phiz as that.
10077{_}574{_}Yet he fought like a tiger up in the mountains.
10077{_}575{_}Of course, the war was a bad business,--and the taxes--whew!
10077{_}576{_}Last summer things were smashed generally, and when Will (his brother) sailed in Sherman's expedition, it was a blue day enough: how his mother and the girls did carry on!
10077{_}577{_}(Nabbes and Will supported the family, by the way; and Nabbes, inside of his slang, billiards, etc., was a good, soft-hearted fellow.)
10077{_}578{_}However, the country was looking up now.
10077{_}579{_}There were our victories,--and his own salary was raised.
10077{_}580{_}Will was snug down at Port Royal,--sent the girls home some confoundedly pretty jewelry; they were as busy as bees, knitting socks, and--What, the Devil!
10077{_}581{_}were we to be ridden over rough-shod by Davis and his crew?
10077{_}582{_}Northern brain and muscle were toughest, and let water find its own level.
10077{_}583{_}So he tore out a fly-leaf from the big Bible, and jotted down notes of the meeting,--"An outpouring of the loyal heart of West Virginia,"--and yawned, ready for bed, contented with the world, himself, and God.
10077{_}584{_}Dyke touched Palmer's arm.
10077{_}585{_}"Lor', Capt'n," he whispered, "ef thar a'n't old Scofield!
10077{_}586{_}'n the back o' th' house, watchin' you.
10077{_}587{_}Son killed at Manassas,--George,--d' ye know?"
10077{_}588{_}"I know."
10077{_}589{_}"Danged ef I don't respect Secesh like them," broke out Dyke.
10077{_}590{_}"Ye'll not sin his soul with a test-oath.
10077{_}591{_}Thar's grit thar.
10077{_}592{_}Well, God help us!"
10077{_}593{_}Palmer stepped down from the pulpit; but the old man, seeing him coming, turned and shouldered his way out of the crowd, his haggard face blood-red.
10077{_}594{_}"What'll the old chap say to Gaunt's enlistin'?"
10077{_}595{_}said Dyke.
10077{_}596{_}"Gaunt in?
10077{_}597{_}Bully for the parson!"
10077{_}598{_}said Squire Pratt.
10077{_}599{_}"Parson 'listed?"
10077{_}600{_}said the reporter.
10077{_}601{_}"They and the women led off in this war.
10077{_}602{_}I'm glad of it,--brings out the pith in 'em."
10077{_}603{_}"I dunno," said Dyke, looking round.
10077{_}604{_}"Gaunt's name brought in a dozen; but----It's a dirty business, the war.
10077{_}605{_}I wish 'n somebody's hands hed stayed clean of it."
10077{_}606{_}"It's the Lord's work," said Pratt, with a twang, being a class-leader.
10077{_}607{_}"Ye-s?
10077{_}608{_}So 'ud Bishop Polk say.
10077{_}609{_}Got a different Lord down thar?
10077{_}610{_}'S likely.
10077{_}611{_}Henry Wise used to talk of the 'God of Virginia.'"
10077{_}612{_}"Was a fellow," said Nabbes, nursing one foot, "that set me easy about my soul, and the thing.
10077{_}613{_}A chaplain in Congress: after we took down that bitter Mason--and--Slidell pill, it was.
10077{_}614{_}Prayed to Jesus to keep us safe until our vengeance on England was ripe,--to 'aid us through the patient watch and vigil long of him who treasures up a wrong.'
10077{_}615{_}Old boy, thinks I, if that's Christianity, it's cheap.
10077{_}616{_}I'll take stock in it.
10077{_}617{_}Going at half-price, I think."
10077{_}618{_}"I am tired of this cant of Christians refusing to join in the war," said Palmer, impatiently.
10077{_}619{_}"God allows it; it helps His plans."
10077{_}620{_}"Humph!
10077{_}621{_}So did Judas," muttered Dyke, shrewdly.
10077{_}622{_}"Well, I a'n't a purfessor myself.--Boys, come along!
10077{_}623{_}Drum-call time.
10077{_}624{_}You're in luck.
10077{_}625{_}We'll have work afore mornin',--an' darned ef you sha'n't be in it, in spite of rules!"
10077{_}626{_}When the recruits went out, the meeting broke up.
10077{_}627{_}Palmer put on his hat, and made his way out of a side-door into the snow-covered field about the church, glancing at his watch as he went.
10077{_}628{_}He had but little time to spare.
10077{_}629{_}The Federal camp lay on a distant hill-side below Romney: through the dim winter shadows he could see points of light shifting from tent to tent; a single bugle-call had shrilled through the mountains once or twice; the regiments ordered for the attack were under arms now, he concluded.
10077{_}630{_}They had a long march before them: the Gap, where the Confederate band were concealed, lay sixteen miles distant.
10077{_}631{_}Unless the Union troops succeeded in surprising the Rebels, the fight, Palmer knew, would be desperate; the position they held was almost impregnable, --camped behind a steep gash in the mountain: a handful of men could hold it against Dunning's whole brigade, unshielded, bare.
10077{_}632{_}A surprise was almost impossible in these mountains, where Rebel guerrillas lurked behind every tree, and every woman in the village-shanties was ready to risk limbs or life as a Rebel spy.
10077{_}633{_}Thus far, however, he thought this movement had been kept secret: even the men did not know where they were going.
10077{_}634{_}Crossing the field hurriedly, he saw two men talking eagerly behind a thorn-bush.
10077{_}635{_}One of them, turning, came towards him, his hat slouched over his face.
10077{_}636{_}It was Scofield.
10077{_}637{_}As he came into the clear starlight, Palmer recognized the thick-set, sluggish figure and haggard face, and waited for him,--with a quick remembrance of long summer days, when he and George, boys together, had looked on this man as the wisest and strongest, sitting at his side digging worms or making yellow flies for him to fish in the Big Cacapon,--how they would have the delicate broiled trout for supper,--how Dode was a chubby little puss then, with white apron and big brown eyes, choosing to sit on his lap when they went to the table, and putting her hand slyly into his coffee.
10077{_}638{_}An odd thing to think of then and there!
10077{_}639{_}George lay stiff now, with a wooden board only at his head to tell that he once lived.
10077{_}640{_}The thoughts struck through Palmer's brain in the waiting moment, making his hand unsteady as he held it out to the old man.
10077{_}641{_}"Uncle Scofield!
10077{_}642{_}Is the war to come between you and me?
10077{_}643{_}For George's sake!
10077{_}644{_}I saw him at Harper's Ferry before--before Manassas.
10077{_}645{_}We were no less friends then than ever before."
10077{_}646{_}The old man's eyes had glared defiance at Palmer under their gray brows when he faced him, but his big bony hand kept fumbling nervously with his cravat.
10077{_}647{_}"Yes, Dougl's.
10077{_}648{_}I didn't want to meet yer.
10077{_}649{_}Red an' white's my colors,--red an' white, so help me God!"
10077{_}650{_}"I know," said Palmer, quietly.
10077{_}651{_}There was a silence,--the men looking steadily at each other.
10077{_}652{_}"Ye saw George?"
10077{_}653{_}the old man said, his eyes falling.
10077{_}654{_}"Yes.
10077{_}655{_}At Harper's Ferry.
10077{_}656{_}I was making my way through the Confederate lines; George took me over, risking his own life to do it, then reported himself under arrest.
10077{_}657{_}He did not lose his commission; your general was just"----  Scofield's face worked.
10077{_}658{_}"That was like my boy!
10077{_}659{_}Thar's not a grandfather he hes in the country whar he's gone to that would believe one of our blood could do a mean thing!
10077{_}660{_}The Scofields ar'n't well larned, but they've true honor, Dougl's Palmer!"
10077{_}661{_}Palmer's eyes lighted.
10077{_}662{_}Men of the old lion-breed know each other in spite of dress or heirship of opinion.
10077{_}663{_}"Ye've been to th' house to-night, boy?"
10077{_}664{_}said the old man, his voice softened.
10077{_}665{_}"Yes?
10077{_}666{_}That was right.
10077{_}667{_}Ye've truer notions nor me.
10077{_}668{_}I went away so 's not till meet yer.
10077{_}669{_}I'm sorry for it.
10077{_}670{_}George's gone, Dougl's, but he'd be glad till think you an' me was the same as ever,--he would!"
10077{_}671{_}He held out his hand.
10077{_}672{_}Something worthy the name of man in each met in the grasp, that no blood spilled could foul or embitter.
10077{_}673{_}They walked across the field together, the old man leaning his hand on Palmer's shoulder as if for support, though he did not need it.
10077{_}674{_}He had been used to walk so with George.
10077{_}675{_}This was his boy's friend: that thought filled and warmed his heart so utterly that he forgot his hand rested on a Federal uniform.
10077{_}676{_}Palmer was strangely silent.
10077{_}677{_}"I saw Theodora," he said at last, gravely.
10077{_}678{_}Scofield started at the tone, looked at him keenly, some new thought breaking in on him, frightening, troubling him.
10077{_}679{_}He did not answer; they crossed the broad field, coming at last to the hill-road.
10077{_}680{_}The old man spoke at last, with an effort.
10077{_}681{_}"You an' my little girl are friends, did you mean, Dougl's?
10077{_}682{_}The war didn't come between ye?"
10077{_}683{_}"Nothing shall come between us,"--quietly, his eye full upon the old man's.
10077{_}684{_}The story of a life lay in the look.
10077{_}685{_}Scofield met it questioningly, almost solemnly.
10077{_}686{_}It was no time for explanation.
10077{_}687{_}He pushed his trembling hand through his stubby gray hair.
10077{_}688{_}"Well, well, Dougl's.
10077{_}689{_}These days is harrd.
10077{_}690{_}But it'll come right!
10077{_}691{_}God knows all."
10077{_}692{_}The road was empty now,--lay narrow and bare down the hill; the moon had set, and the snow-clouds were graying heavily the pale light above.
10077{_}693{_}Only the sharp call of a discordant trumpet broke the solitude and dumbness of the hills.
10077{_}694{_}A lonesome, foreboding night.
10077{_}695{_}The old man rested his hand on the fence, choking down an uncertain groan now and then, digging into the snow with his foot, while Palmer watched him.
10077{_}696{_}"I must bid yer good-bye, Dougl's," he said at last.
10077{_}697{_}"I've a long tramp afore me to-night.
10077{_}698{_}Mebbe worse.
10077{_}699{_}Mayhap I mayn't see you agin; men can't hev a grip on the next hour, these days.
10077{_}700{_}I'm glad we 're friends.
10077{_}701{_}Whatever comes afore mornin', I'm glad o' that!"
10077{_}702{_}"Have you no more to say to me?"
10077{_}703{_}"Yes, Dougl's,--'s for my little girl,--ef so be as I should foller my boy sometime, I'd wish you'd be friends to Dode, Dougl's.
10077{_}704{_}Yes!
10077{_}705{_}I would,"--hesitating, something wet oozing from his small black eye, and losing itself in the snuffy wrinkles.
10077{_}706{_}Palmer was touched.
10077{_}707{_}It was a hard struggle with pain that had wrung out that tear.
10077{_}708{_}The old man held his hand a minute, then turned to the road.
10077{_}709{_}"Whichever of us sees Geordy first kin tell him t' other's livin' a true-grit honest life, call him Yankee or Virginian,--an' that's enough said!
10077{_}710{_}So good bye, Dougl's!"
10077{_}711{_}Palmer mounted his horse and galloped off to the camp, the old man plodding steadily down the road.
10077{_}712{_}When the echo of the horse's hoofs had ceased, a lean gangling figure came from out of the field-brush, and met him.
10077{_}713{_}"Why, David boy!
10077{_}714{_}whar were ye to-night?"
10077{_}715{_}Scofield's voice had grown strangely tender in the last hour.
10077{_}716{_}Gaunt hesitated.
10077{_}717{_}He had not the moral courage to tell the old man he had enlisted.
10077{_}718{_}"I waited.
10077{_}719{_}I must air the church,--it is polluted with foul smells."
10077{_}720{_}Scofield laughed to himself at David's "whimsey," but he halted, going with the young man as he strode across the field.
10077{_}721{_}He had a dull foreboding of the end of the night's battle: before he went to it, he clung with a womanish affection to anything belonging to his home, as this Gaunt did.
10077{_}722{_}He had not thought the poor young man was so dear to him, until now, as he jogged along beside him, thinking that before morning he might be lying dead at the Gap.
10077{_}723{_}How many people would care?
10077{_}724{_}David would, and Dode, and old Bone.
10077{_}725{_}Gaunt hurried in,--he ought to be in camp, but he could not leave the house of God polluted all night,--opening the windows, even carrying the flag outside.
10077{_}726{_}The emblem of freedom, of course,--but----He hardly knew why he did it.
10077{_}727{_}There were flags on every Methodist chapel, almost: the sect had thrown itself into the war _con amore_.
10077{_}728{_}But Gaunt had fallen into that sect by mistake; his animal nature was too weak for it: as for his feeling about the church, he had just that faint shade of Pantheism innate in him that would have made a good Episcopalian.
10077{_}729{_}The planks of the floor were more to him than other planks; something else than sunshine had often shone in to him through the little panes,--he touched them gently; he walked softly over the rag-carpet on the aisle.
10077{_}730{_}The LORD was in His holy temple.
10077{_}731{_}With another thought close behind that, of the time when the church was built, more than a year ago; what a happy, almost jolly time they had, the members giving the timber, and making a sort of frolic of putting it up, in the afternoons after harvest.
10077{_}732{_}They were all in one army or the other now: some of them in Blue's Gap.
10077{_}733{_}He would help ferret them out in the morning.
10077{_}734{_}He shivered, with the old doubt tugging fiercely at his heart.
10077{_}735{_}Was he right?
10077{_}736{_}The war was one of God's great judgments, but was it _his_ place to be in it?
10077{_}737{_}It was too late to question now.
10077{_}738{_}He went up into the pulpit, taking out the Bible that lay on the shelf, lighting a candle, glancing uneasily at the old man on the steps.
10077{_}739{_}He never had feared to meet his eye before.
10077{_}740{_}He turned to the fly-leaf, holding it to the candle.
10077{_}741{_}What odd fancy made him want to read the uncouth, blotted words written there?
10077{_}742{_}He knew them well enough.
10077{_}743{_}"To my Dear frend, David Gaunt.
10077{_}744{_}May, 1860. the Lord be Betwien mee And thee.
10077{_}745{_}J.
10077{_}746{_}Scofield."
10077{_}747{_}It was two years since he had given it to Gaunt, just after George had been so ill with cholera, and David had nursed him through with it.
10077{_}748{_}Gaunt fancied that nursing had made the hearts of both son and father more tender than all his sermons.
10077{_}749{_}He used to pray with them in the evenings as George grew better, hardly able to keep from weeping like a woman, for George was very dear to him.
10077{_}750{_}Afterwards the old man came to church more regularly, and George had quit swearing, and given up card-playing.
10077{_}751{_}He remembered the evening when the old man gave him the Bible.
10077{_}752{_}He had been down in Wheeling, and when he came home brought it out to Gaunt in the old corn-field, wrapped up in his best red bandanna handkerchief,--his face growing red and pale.
10077{_}753{_}"It's the Book, David.
10077{_}754{_}I thort ef you'd use this one till preach from.
10077{_}755{_}Mayhap it wouldn't be right till take it from a sinner like me, but--I thort I'd like it, somehow,"--showing him the fly-leaf.
10077{_}756{_}"I writ this,--ef it would be true,--what I writ,--'The Lord he between me and thee'?"
10077{_}757{_}Gaunt passed his fingers now over the misspelled words softly as he would stroke a dead face.
10077{_}758{_}Then he came out, putting out the candle, and buttoning the Bible inside of his coat.
10077{_}759{_}Scofield waited for him on the steps.
10077{_}760{_}Some trouble was in the old fellow's face, Gaunt thought, which he could not fathom.
10077{_}761{_}His coarse voice choked every now and then, and his eyes looked as though he never hoped to see the church or Gaunt again.
10077{_}762{_}"Heh, David!"
10077{_}763{_}with a silly laugh.
10077{_}764{_}"You'll think me humorsome, boy, but I hev an odd fancy."
10077{_}765{_}He stopped abruptly.
10077{_}766{_}"What is it?"
10077{_}767{_}"It's lonesome here,"--looking around vaguely.
10077{_}768{_}"God seems near here on the hills, d' ye think?
10077{_}769{_}David, I'm goin' a bit out on the road to-night, an' life's uncertain these times.
10077{_}770{_}Whiles I think I might never be back to see Dode agin,--or you.
10077{_}771{_}David, you're nearer to Him than me; you brought me to Him, you know.
10077{_}772{_}S'pose,--you'll think me foolish now,--ef we said a bit prayer here afore I go; what d'ye think?
10077{_}773{_}Heh?"
10077{_}774{_}Gaunt was startled.
10077{_}775{_}Somehow to-night he did not feel as if God was near on the hills, as Scofield thought.
10077{_}776{_}"I will,"--hesitating.
10077{_}777{_}"Are you going to see Dode first, before you go?"
10077{_}778{_}"Dode?
10077{_}779{_}Don't speak of her, boy!
10077{_}780{_}I'm sick!
10077{_}781{_}Kneel down an' pray,--the Lord's Prayer,--that's enough,--mother taught me that,"--baring his gray head, while Gaunt, his worn face turned to the sky, said the old words over.
10077{_}782{_}"Forgive," he muttered,--"resist not evil,"--some fragments vexing his brain.
10077{_}783{_}"Did He mean that?
10077{_}784{_}David boy?
10077{_}785{_}Did He mean His people to trust in God to right them as He did?
10077{_}786{_}Pah!
10077{_}787{_}times is different now,"--pulling his hat over his forehead to go.
10077{_}788{_}"Good bye, David!"
10077{_}789{_}"Where are you going?"
10077{_}790{_}"I don't mind tellin' you,--you'll keep it.
10077{_}791{_}Bone's bringin' a horse yonder to the road.
10077{_}792{_}I'm goin' to warn the boys to be ready, an' help 'em,--at the Gap, you know?"
10077{_}793{_}"The Gap?
10077{_}794{_}Merciful God, no!"
10077{_}795{_}cried Gaunt.
10077{_}796{_}"Go back"----  The words stopped in his throat.
10077{_}797{_}What if he met this man there?
10077{_}798{_}Scofield looked at him, bewildered.
10077{_}799{_}"Thar's no danger," he said, calmly.
10077{_}800{_}"Yer nerves are weak.
10077{_}801{_}But yer love for me's true, David.
10077{_}802{_}That's sure,"--with a smile.
10077{_}803{_}"But I've got to warn the boys.
10077{_}804{_}Good bye,"--hesitating, his face growing red.
10077{_}805{_}"Ye'll mind, ef anything should happen,--what I writ in the Book,--once,--'The Lord be between me an' thee,' dead or alive?
10077{_}806{_}Them's good, friendly words.
10077{_}807{_}Good bye!
10077{_}808{_}God bless you, boy!"
10077{_}809{_}Gaunt wrung his hand, and watched him as he turned to the road.
10077{_}810{_}He saw Bone meet him, leading a horse.
10077{_}811{_}As the old man mounted, he turned, and, seeing Gaunt, nodded cheerfully, and going down the hill began to whistle.
10077{_}812{_}"Ef I should never come back, he kin tell Dode I hed a light heart at th' last," he thought.
10077{_}813{_}But when he was out of hearing, the whistle stopped, and he put spurs to the horse.
10077{_}814{_}Counting the hours, the minutes,--a turbid broil of thought in his brain, of Dode sitting alone, of George and his murderers, "stiffening his courage,"--right and wrong mixing each other inextricably together.
10077{_}815{_}If, now and then, a shadow crossed him of the meek Nazarene leaving this word to His followers, that, let the world do as it would, _they_ should resist not evil, he thrust it back.
10077{_}816{_}It did not suit to-day.
10077{_}817{_}Hours passed.
10077{_}818{_}The night crept on towards morning, colder, stiller.
10077{_}819{_}Faint bars of gray fell on the stretch of hill-tops, broad and pallid.
10077{_}820{_}The shaggy peaks blanched whiter in it.
10077{_}821{_}You could hear from the road-bushes the chirp of a snow-bird, wakened by the tramp of his horse, or the flutter of its wings.
10077{_}822{_}Overhead, the stars disappeared, like flakes of fire going out; the sky came nearer, tinged with healthier blue.
10077{_}823{_}He could see the mountain where the Gap was, close at hand, but a few miles distant.
10077{_}824{_}He had met no pickets: he believed the whole Confederate camp there was asleep.
10077{_}825{_}And behind him, on the road he had just passed, trailing up the side of a hill, was a wavering, stealthy line, creeping slowly nearer every minute,--the gray columns under Dunning.
10077{_}826{_}The old man struck the rowels into his horse,--the boys would be murdered in their sleep!
10077{_}827{_}The road was rutted deep: the horse, an old village hack, lumbered along, stumbling at every step.
10077{_}828{_}"Ef my old bones was what they used to be, I'd best trust them," he muttered.
10077{_}829{_}Another hour was over; there were but two miles before him to the Gap: but the old mare panted and balked at every ditch across the road.
10077{_}830{_}The Federal force was near; even the tap of their drum had ceased long since; their march was as silent as a tiger's spring.
10077{_}831{_}Close behind,--closer every minute!
10077{_}832{_}He pulled the rein savagely,--why could not the dumb brute know that life and death waited on her foot?
10077{_}833{_}The poor beast's eye lightened.
10077{_}834{_}She gathered her whole strength, sprang forward, struck upon a glaze of ice, and fell.
10077{_}835{_}The old man dragged himself out.
10077{_}836{_}"Poor old Jin!
10077{_}837{_}ye did what ye could!"
10077{_}838{_}he said.
10077{_}839{_}He was lamed by the fall.
10077{_}840{_}It was no time to think of that; he hobbled on, the cold drops of sweat oozing out on his face from pain.
10077{_}841{_}Reaching the bridge that crosses the stream there, he glanced back.
10077{_}842{_}He could not see the Federal troops, but he heard the dull march of their regiments,--like some giant's tread, slow, muffled in snow.
10077{_}843{_}Closer,--closer every minute!
10077{_}844{_}His heavy boots clogged with snow; the pain exhausted even his thick lungs,--they breathed heavily; he climbed the narrow ridge of ground that ran parallel with the road, and hurried on.
10077{_}845{_}Half an hour more, and he would save them!
10077{_}846{_}A cold, stirless air: Gaunt panted in it.
10077{_}847{_}Was there ever night so silent?
10077{_}848{_}Following his lead, came the long column, a dark, even-moving mass, shirred with steel.
10077{_}849{_}Sometimes he could catch glimpses of some vivid point in the bulk: a hand, moving nervously to the sword's hilt; faces,--sensual, or vapid, or royal, side by side, but sharpened alike by a high purpose, with shut jaws, and keen, side-glancing eyes.
10077{_}850{_}He was in advance of them, with one other man,--Dyke.
10077{_}851{_}Dyke took him, as knowing the country best, and being a trustworthy guide.
10077{_}852{_}So this was work!
10077{_}853{_}True work for a man.
10077{_}854{_}Marching hour after hour through the solitary night, he had time to think.
10077{_}855{_}Dyke talked to him but little: said once, "P'raps 't was as well the parsons had wakened up, and was mixin' with other folks.
10077{_}856{_}Gettin' into camp 'ud show 'em original sin, he guessed.
10077{_}857{_}Not but what this war-work brought out good in a man.
10077{_}858{_}Makes 'em, or breaks 'em, ginerally."
10077{_}859{_}And then was silent.
10077{_}860{_}Gaunt caught the words.
10077{_}861{_}Yes,--it was better preachers should lay off the prestige of the cloth, and rough it like their Master, face to face with men.
10077{_}862{_}There would be fewer despicable shams among them.
10077{_}863{_}But _this?_--clutching the loaded pistol in his hand.
10077{_}864{_}Thinking of Cromwell and Hedley Vicars.
10077{_}865{_}Freedom!
10077{_}866{_}It was a nobler cause than theirs.
10077{_}867{_}But a Face was before him, white, thorn-crowned, bent watchful over the world.
10077{_}868{_}He was sent of Jesus.
10077{_}869{_}To do what?
10077{_}870{_}Preach peace by murder?
10077{_}871{_}What said his Master?
10077{_}872{_}"That _ye_ resist not evil."
10077{_}873{_}Bah!
10077{_}874{_}Palmer said the doctrine of nonresistance was whining cant.
10077{_}875{_}As long as human nature was the same, right and wrong would be left to the arbitrament of brute force.
10077{_}876{_}And yet--was not Christianity a diviner breath than this passing through the ages?
10077{_}877{_}"Ye are the light of the world."
10077{_}878{_}Even the "roughs" sneered at the fighting parsons.
10077{_}879{_}It was too late to think now.
10077{_}880{_}He pushed back his thin yellow hair, his homesick eyes wandering upwards, his mouth growing dry and parched.
10077{_}881{_}They were nearing the mountain now.
10077{_}882{_}Dawn was coming.
10077{_}883{_}The gray sky heated and glowed into inner deeps of rose; the fresh morning air sprang from its warm nest somewhere, and came to meet them, like some one singing a heartsome song under his breath.
10077{_}884{_}The faces of the columns looked more rigid, paler, in the glow: men facing death have no time for fresh morning thoughts.
10077{_}885{_}They were within a few rods of the Gap.
10077{_}886{_}As yet there was no sign of sentinel,--not even the click of a musket was heard.
10077{_}887{_}"They sleep like the dead," muttered Dyke.
10077{_}888{_}"We'll be on them in five minutes more."
10077{_}889{_}Gaunt, keeping step with him, pressing up the hill, shivered.
10077{_}890{_}He thought he saw blood on his hands.
10077{_}891{_}Why, this was work!
10077{_}892{_}His whole body throbbed as with one pulse.
10077{_}893{_}Behind him, a long way, came the column; his quickened nerves felt the slow beat of their tread, like the breathing of some great animal.
10077{_}894{_}Crouching in a stubble-field at the road-side he saw a negro,--a horse at a little distance.
10077{_}895{_}It was Bone; he had followed his master: the thought passing vaguely before him without meaning.
10077{_}896{_}On!
10077{_}897{_}on!

10077{_}925{_}For a moment the silence was unbroken.

10077{_}935{_}The Federal victory was complete.
10077{_}936{_}The Rebel forces were carried off prisoners to Romney.
10077{_}937{_}How many, on either side, were lost, as in every battle of our civil war, no one can tell: it is better, perhaps, we do not know.
10077{_}938{_}The Federal column did not return in an unbroken mass as they went.
10077{_}939{_}There were wounded and dying among them; some vacant places.
10077{_}940{_}Besides, they had work to do on their road back: the Rebels had been sheltered in the farmers' houses near; the "nest must be cleaned out": every homestead but two from Romney to the Gap was laid in ashes.
10077{_}941{_}It was not a pleasant sight for the officers to see women and children flying half-naked and homeless through the snow, nor did they think it would strengthen the Union sentiment; but what could they do?
10077{_}942{_}As great atrocities as these were committed by the Rebels.
10077{_}943{_}The war, as Palmer said, was a savage necessity.
10077{_}944{_}When the fight was nearly over, the horse which Palmer rode broke from the _melee_ and rushed back to the road.

10077{_}951{_}He hoped to God the Union cause would triumph.

10077{_}1017{_}She knew what the old man had been to her--now!
10077{_}1018{_}There was not a homely way he had of showing his unutterable pride and love for his little girl that did not wring her very soul.
10077{_}1019{_}She had always loved him; but she knew now how much warmer and brighter his rough life might have been, if she had chosen to make it so.
10077{_}1020{_}There was not a cross word of hers, nor an angry look, that she did not remember with a bitterness that made her sick as death.
10077{_}1021{_}If she could but know he forgave her!

10077{_}1023{_}She loathed herself, her coldness, her want of love to him,--to all the world.
10077{_}1024{_}If she could only tell him she loved him, once more!--hiding her face in his breast, wishing she could lie there as cold and still as he, whispering, continually, "Father!

10077{_}1026{_}Could he not hear?
10077{_}1027{_}When they took him away, she did not cry nor faint.
10077{_}1028{_}When trouble stabbed Dode to the quick, she was one of those people who do not ask for help, but go alone, like a hurt deer, until the wound heals or kills.
10077{_}1029{_}This was a loss for life.
10077{_}1030{_}Of course, this throbbing pain would grieve itself down; but in all the years to come no one would take just the place her old father had left vacant.
10077{_}1031{_}Husband and child might be dearer, but she would never be "Dody" to any one again.
10077{_}1032{_}She shut the loss up in her own heart.
10077{_}1033{_}She never named him afterwards.
10077{_}1034{_}It was a cold winter's evening, that, after the funeral.
10077{_}1035{_}The January wind came up with a sharp, dreary sough into the defiles of the hills, crusting over the snow-sweeps with a glaze of ice that glittered in the pearly sunlight, clear up the rugged peaks.
10077{_}1036{_}There, at the edge of them, the snow fretted and arched and fell back in curling foam-waves with hints of delicate rose-bloom in their white shining.
10077{_}1037{_}The trees, that had stood all winter bare and patient, lifting up their dumb arms in dreary supplication, suddenly, to-day, clothed themselves, every trunk and limb and twig, in flashing ice, that threw back into the gray air the royal greeting of a thousand splendid dyes, violet, amber, and crimson,--to show God they did not need to wait for summer days to praise Him.
10077{_}1038{_}A cold afternoon: even the seeds hid in the mould down below the snow were chilled to the heart, and thought they surely could not live the winter out: the cows, when Bone went out drearily to feed them by himself, were watching the thin, frozen breath steaming from their nostrils with tears in their eyes, he thought.
10077{_}1039{_}A cold day: cold for the sick and wounded soldiers that were jolted in ambulances down the mountain-roads through its creeping hours.
10077{_}1040{_}For the Federal troops had evacuated Romney.
10077{_}1041{_}The Rebel forces, under Jackson, had nearly closed around the mountain-camp before they were discovered: they were twenty thousand strong.
10077{_}1042{_}Lander's force was but a handful in comparison: he escaped with them for their lives that day, leaving the town and the hills in full possession of the Confederates.
10077{_}1043{_}A bleak, heartless day: coldest of all for Dode, lying on the floor of her little room.
10077{_}1044{_}How wide and vacant the world looked to her!
10077{_}1045{_}What could she do there?
10077{_}1046{_}Why was she born?
10077{_}1047{_}She must show her Master to others,--of course; but--she was alone: everybody she loved had been taken from her.
10077{_}1048{_}She wished that she were dead.
10077{_}1049{_}She lay there, trying to pray, now and then,--motionless, like some death in life; the gray sunlight looking in at her, in a wondering way.
10077{_}1050{_}It was quite contented to be gray and cold, till summer came.
10077{_}1051{_}Out in the little kitchen, the day had warmed up wonderfully.
10077{_}1052{_}Dode's Aunt Perrine, a widow of thirty years' standing, had come over to "see to things durin' this murnful affliction."
10077{_}1053{_}As she had brought her hair-trunk and bonnet-box, it was probable her stay would be indefinite.
10077{_}1054{_}Dode was conscious of her as she would be of an attack of nettle-rash.
10077{_}1055{_}Mrs. Perrine and her usual burying-colleague, "Mis' Browst," had gotten up a snug supper of fried oysters, and between that and the fresh relish of horror from the funeral were in a high state of enjoyment.
10077{_}1056{_}Aunt Perrine, having officiated as chief mourner that very morning, was not disposed to bear her honors meekly.
10077{_}1057{_}"It was little Jane Browst knew of sorrer.
10077{_}1058{_}With eight gells well married,--_well_ married, Jane,--deny it, ef you can,--what can you know of my feelins this day?

10077{_}1061{_}But who 'll take his place to me?

10077{_}1077{_}She always came out best at last, though her hair was toffy-colored and her eyes a washed-out blue, and Aunt Perrine was of the color of a mild Indian.
10077{_}1078{_}Two of Mis' Browst's sons-in-law had been "burned out" by the Yankees; another was in the Union army: these trump-cards of misery she did now so produce and flourish and weep over that she utterly routed the enemy, reduced her to stolid silence.

10077{_}1180{_}Night would soon be here, and he lay stiff-cold beneath the snow.
10077{_}1181{_}Not dead: her heart told her that imperiously from the first.
10077{_}1182{_}But there was not one instant to lose.
10077{_}1183{_}"I cannot wait for you, Uncle Bone.
10077{_}1184{_}I must go alone."
10077{_}1185{_}"Debbil de step!
10077{_}1186{_}I'll take yer 'cross fields ter Gentry's, an' ride on myself."
10077{_}1187{_}"You could not find him.
10077{_}1188{_}No one could find him but me."
10077{_}1189{_}Something possessed the girl, other than her common self.
10077{_}1190{_}She pushed his hand gently from the reins, and left him.
10077{_}1191{_}Bone wrung his hands.
10077{_}1192{_}"'N' de guerrillas,--'n' de rest o' de incarnate debbils!"
10077{_}1193{_}She knew that.
10077{_}1194{_}Dode was no heroine,--a miserable coward.
10077{_}1195{_}There was not a black stump of a tree by the road-side, nor the rustle of a squirrel in the trees, that did not make her heart jump and throb against her bodice.
10077{_}1196{_}Her horse climbed the rocky path slowly.
10077{_}1197{_}I told you the girl thought her Helper was alive, and very near.
10077{_}1198{_}She did to-night.
10077{_}1199{_}She thought He was beside her in this lonesome road, and knew she would be safe.
10077{_}1200{_}She felt as if she could take hold of His very hand.
10077{_}1201{_}It grew darker: the mountains of snow glowered wan like the dead kings in Hades; the sweeps of dark forests whispered some broken mysterious word, as she passed; sometimes, in a sudden opening, she could see on a far hill-side the red fires of a camp.
10077{_}1202{_}She could not help the sick feeling in her throat, nor make her hand steady; but the more alone she was, the nearer He came,--the pale face of the Nazarene, who loved His mother and Mary, who took the little children in His arms before He blessed them.
10077{_}1203{_}Nearer than ever before; so she was not afraid to tell Him, as she went, how she had suffered that day, and that she loved this man who lay dying under the snow: to ask that she might find him.
10077{_}1204{_}A great gulf lay between them.
10077{_}1205{_}Would _He_ go with her, if she crossed it?
10077{_}1206{_}She knew He would.
10077{_}1207{_}A strange peace came to the girl.
10077{_}1208{_}She untied her hood and pushed it back, that her whole head might feel the still air.
10077{_}1209{_}How pure it was!
10077{_}1210{_}God was in it,--in all.
10077{_}1211{_}The mountains, the sky, the armies yonder, her own heart, and his under the snow, rested in Him, like motes in the sunshine.
10077{_}1212{_}The moon, rising behind a bank of cloud, threw patches of light now and then across the path: the girl's head, as she rode through them, came into quick relief.
10077{_}1213{_}No saint's face,--a very woman's, its pale, reserved beauty unstrung with pain, her bosom full of earthly love, but in her eyes that look which Mary must have given, when, after she thought her Lord was dead, He called her, "Mary!"
10077{_}1214{_}and she, looking up, said, "Master!"
10077{_}1215{_}She had reached the highway at last.
10077{_}1216{_}She could see where, some distance yet beyond, the gully struck black across the snow-covered fields.
10077{_}1217{_}The road ran above it, zigzag along the hill-side.
10077{_}1218{_}She thought, as her horse galloped up the path, she could see the very spot where Douglas was lying.
10077{_}1219{_}Not dead,--she knew he was not dead!
10077{_}1220{_}She came to it now.
10077{_}1221{_}How deathly still it was!
10077{_}1222{_}As she tied the horse to the fence, and climbed down the precipice through the snow, she was dimly conscious that the air was warmer, that the pure moonlight was about her, genial, hopeful.
10077{_}1223{_}A startled snow-bird chirped to her, as she passed.
10077{_}1224{_}Why, it was a happy promise!
10077{_}1225{_}Why should it not be happy?
10077{_}1226{_}He was not dead, and she had leave to come to him.
10077{_}1227{_}Yet, before she gained the level field, the pulse in her body was weak and sick, and her eyes were growing blind.
10077{_}1228{_}She did not see him.
10077{_}1229{_}Half covered by snow, she found his gray horse, dead, killed by the fall.
10077{_}1230{_}Palmer was gone.
10077{_}1231{_}The gully was covered with muddy ice; there was a split in it, and underneath, the black water curdled and frothed.
10077{_}1232{_}Had he fallen there?
10077{_}1233{_}Was that thing that rose and fell in the roots of the old willow his dead hand?
10077{_}1234{_}There was a floating gleam of yellow in the water,--it looked like hair.
10077{_}1235{_}Dode put her hand to her hot breast, shut her dry lips.
10077{_}1236{_}He was not dead!
10077{_}1237{_}God could not lie to her!
10077{_}1238{_}Stooping, she went over the ground again, an unbroken waste of white: until, close to the water's edge, she found the ginseng-weeds torn and trampled down.
10077{_}1239{_}She never afterwards smelt their unclean, pungent odor, without a sudden pang of the smothered pain of this night coming back to her.
10077{_}1240{_}She knelt, and found foot-marks,--one booted and spurred.
10077{_}1241{_}She knew it: what was there he had touched that she did not know?
10077{_}1242{_}He was alive: she did not cry out at this, or laugh, as her soul went up to God,--only thrust her hand deep into the snow where his foot had been, with a quick, fierce tenderness, blushing as she drew it back, as if she had forgotten herself, and from her heart caressed him.
10077{_}1243{_}She heard a sound at the other side of a bend in the hill, a low drone, like somebody mumbling a hymn.
10077{_}1244{_}She pushed her way through the thicket: the moon did not shine there; there was a dark crevice in the hill, where some farmer's boy had built a shed.
10077{_}1245{_}There was a fire in it, now, smouldering, as though whoever made it feared its red light would be seen by the distant pickets.
10077{_}1246{_}Coming up to it, she stood in the door-way.
10077{_}1247{_}Douglas Palmer lay on a heap of blankets on the ground: she could not see his face, for a lank, slothful figure was stooping over him, chafing his head.
10077{_}1248{_}It was Gaunt.
10077{_}1249{_}Dode went in, and knelt down beside the wounded man,--quietly: it seemed to her natural and right she should be there.
10077{_}1250{_}Palmer's eyes were shut, his breathing heavy, uncertain; but his clothes were dried, and his side was bandaged.
10077{_}1251{_}"It was only a flesh-wound," said Gaunt, in his vague way,--"deep, though.
10077{_}1252{_}I knew how to bind it.
10077{_}1253{_}He'll live, Douglas will."
10077{_}1254{_}He did not seem surprised to see the girl.
10077{_}1255{_}Nothing could be so bizarre in the world, that his cloudy, crotchety brain did not accept it, and make a commonplace matter out of it.
10077{_}1256{_}It never occurred to him to wonder how she came there.
10077{_}1257{_}He stood with folded arms, his bony shoulders bolstering up the board wall, watching her as she knelt, her hands on Palmer's pillow, but not touching him.
10077{_}1258{_}Gaunt's lean face had a pitiful look, sometimes,--the look of the child he was in his heart,--hungry, wistful, as though he sought for something, which you might have, perhaps.
10077{_}1259{_}He looked at Dode,--the child of the man that he had killed.
10077{_}1260{_}She did not know that.
10077{_}1261{_}When she came in, he thought of shaking hands with her, as he used to do.
10077{_}1262{_}That could never be again,--never.
10077{_}1263{_}_The man that he had killed?_ Whatever that meant to him, his artist eye took keen note of Dode, as she knelt there, in spite of remorse or pain below: how her noble, delicate head rose from the coarse blue drapery, the dark rings of her curling hair, the pale, clear-cut face, the burning lips, the eyes whose earthly soul was for the man who lay there.
10077{_}1264{_}He knew that, yet he never loved her so fiercely as now,--now, when her father's blood lay between them.
10077{_}1265{_}"Did you find him?"
10077{_}1266{_}she asked, without looking up.
10077{_}1267{_}"I ought to have done it.
10077{_}1268{_}I wish I had done that.
10077{_}1269{_}I wish I had given him his life.
10077{_}1270{_}It was my right."
10077{_}1271{_}One would think she was talking in her sleep.
10077{_}1272{_}"Why was it your right?"
10077{_}1273{_}he asked, quietly.
10077{_}1274{_}"Because I loved him."
10077{_}1275{_}Gaunt raised his hand to his head suddenly.
10077{_}1276{_}"Did you, Dode?
10077{_}1277{_}I had a better right than that.
10077{_}1278{_}Because I hated him."
10077{_}1279{_}"He never harmed you, David Gaunt,"--with as proud composure as that with which a Roman wife would defend her lord.
10077{_}1280{_}"I saved his life.
10077{_}1281{_}Dode, I'm trying to do right: God knows I am.
10077{_}1282{_}But I hated him; he took from me the only thing that would have loved me."
10077{_}1283{_}She looked up timidly, her face growing crimson.
10077{_}1284{_}"I never would have loved you, David."
10077{_}1285{_}"No?
10077{_}1286{_}I'm sorry you told me that, Dode."
10077{_}1287{_}That was all he said.
10077{_}1288{_}He helped her gently, as she arranged the carpets and old blanket under the wounded man; then he went out into the fresh air, saying he did not feel well.
10077{_}1289{_}She was glad that he was gone; Palmer moved uneasily; she wanted his first look all to herself.
10077{_}1290{_}She pushed back his fair hair: what a broad, melancholy forehead lay under it!
10077{_}1291{_}The man wanted something to believe in,--a God in life: you could see that in his face.
10077{_}1292{_}She was to bring it to him: she could not keep the tears back to think that this was so.
10077{_}1293{_}The next minute she laughed in her childish fashion, as she put the brandy to his lips, and the color came to his face.
10077{_}1294{_}He had been physician before; now it was her turn to master and rule.
10077{_}1295{_}He looked up at last, into her eyes, bewildered,--his face struggling to gather sense, distinctness.
10077{_}1296{_}When he spoke, though, it was in his quiet old voice.
10077{_}1297{_}"I have been asleep.
10077{_}1298{_}Where is Gaunt?
10077{_}1299{_}He dressed my side."
10077{_}1300{_}"He is out, sitting on the hill-side."
10077{_}1301{_}"And you are here, Theodora?"
10077{_}1302{_}"Yes, Douglas."
10077{_}1303{_}He was silent.
10077{_}1304{_}He was weak from loss of blood, but his thoughts were sharp, clear as never before.
10077{_}1305{_}The years that were gone of his life seemed clogged into one bulk; how hungry they had been, hard, cruel!
10077{_}1306{_}He never had felt it as now, while he lay helpless, his sultry look reading the woman's eyes bent on his.
10077{_}1307{_}They were pure and restful; love and home waited in them; something beyond,--a peace he could not yet comprehend.
10077{_}1308{_}But this life was not for him,--he remembered that; the girl was nothing to him now: he was not fool enough to taunt himself with false hopes.
10077{_}1309{_}She came there out of pity: any woman would do as much for a wounded man.
10077{_}1310{_}He would never fool himself to be so balked again.
10077{_}1311{_}The loss cut too deep.
10077{_}1312{_}So he forced his face to be cool and critical, while poor Dode waited, innocently wondering that he did not welcome her, pity her now that her father was dead, forgetting that he knew nothing of that.
10077{_}1313{_}For him, he looked at the fire, wondering if the Rebel scouts could see it,--thinking it would not be many days before Lander would dislodge Jackson,--trying to think of anything rather than himself, and the beautiful woman kneeling there.
10077{_}1314{_}Her eyes filled with tears at last, when he did not speak, and she turned away.
10077{_}1315{_}The blood rushed to Palmer's face: surely that was more than pity!
10077{_}1316{_}But he would not tempt her,--he would never vex her soul as he had done before: if she had come to him, as a sister might, because she thought he was dying, he would not taunt her with the old love she had for him.
10077{_}1317{_}"I think I can stand up," he said, cheerfully; "lend me your arm, Theodora."
10077{_}1318{_}Dode's arm was strong-nerved as well as fair; she helped him rise, and stood beside him as he went to the door, for he walked unsteadily.
10077{_}1319{_}He took his hand from her shoulder instantly,--did not look at her: followed with his eye the black line of the fretted hills, the glimmer of the distant watch-fires.
10077{_}1320{_}The path to the West lay through the Rebel camps.
10077{_}1321{_}"It is a long trail out of danger," he said, smiling.
10077{_}1322{_}"You are going?
10077{_}1323{_}I thought you needed rest."
10077{_}1324{_}Calm, icy enough now: he was indifferent to her.
10077{_}1325{_}She knew how to keep the pain down until he was gone.
10077{_}1326{_}"Rest?
10077{_}1327{_}Yes.
10077{_}1328{_}Where did you mean I should find it?"
10077{_}1329{_}--facing her, sudden and keen.
10077{_}1330{_}"Where am I to be sheltered?
10077{_}1331{_}In your home, Theodora?"
10077{_}1332{_}"I thought that.
10077{_}1333{_}I see now that it was a foolish hope, Douglas."
10077{_}1334{_}"How did you hope it?
10077{_}1335{_}What brought you here?"
10077{_}1336{_}--his voice thick, tremulous with passion.
10077{_}1337{_}"Were you going to take me in as a Sister of Charity might some wounded dog?
10077{_}1338{_}Are pity and gratitude all that is left between you and me?"
10077{_}1339{_}She did not answer,--her face pale, unmoving in the moonlight, quietly turned to his.
10077{_}1340{_}These mad heats did not touch her.
10077{_}1341{_}"You may be cold enough to palter with fire that has burned you, Theodora.
10077{_}1342{_}I am not."
10077{_}1343{_}She did not speak.
10077{_}1344{_}"Sooner than have gone to you for sisterly help and comfort, such as you gave just now, I would have frozen in the snow, and been less cold.
10077{_}1345{_}Unless you break down the bar you put between us, I never want to see your face again,--never, living or dead!
10077{_}1346{_}I want no sham farce of friendship between us, benefits given or received: your hand touching mine as it might touch Bone's or David Gaunt's; your voice cooing in my ear as it did just now, cool and friendly.
10077{_}1347{_}It maddened me.

10077{_}1349{_}"I understand you.

10077{_}1352{_}He stopped abruptly, looked at her steadily.
10077{_}1353{_}"Do not taunt me, child!
10077{_}1354{_}I am a blunt man: what words say, they mean, to me.

10077{_}1356{_}She did not speak, drawn back from him in the opposite shadow of the door-way.
10077{_}1357{_}He leaned forward, his breath coming hurried, low.

10077{_}1361{_}"I did come to you.
10077{_}1362{_}Look!
10077{_}1363{_}you put me back: 'There shall be no benefits given or received between us.'"

10077{_}1365{_}--gravely, as a man should speak to a woman, childish trifling thrust aside.
10077{_}1366{_}"How did you mean to take me home?
10077{_}1367{_}As a pure, God-fearing woman should the man she loved?
10077{_}1368{_}Into your heart, into your holiest thought?
10077{_}1369{_}to gather strength from my strength, to make my power your power, your God my God?
10077{_}1370{_}to be one with me?
10077{_}1371{_}Was it so you came?"
10077{_}1372{_}He waited a minute.
10077{_}1373{_}How cold and lonely the night was!
10077{_}1374{_}How near rest and home came to him in this woman standing there!
10077{_}1375{_}Would he lose them?
10077{_}1376{_}One moment more would tell.
10077{_}1377{_}When he spoke again, his voice was lower, feeble.
10077{_}1378{_}"There is a great gulf between you and me, Theodora.
10077{_}1379{_}I know that.
10077{_}1380{_}Will you cross it?
10077{_}1381{_}Will you come to me?"
10077{_}1382{_}She came to him.
10077{_}1383{_}He gathered her into his arms as he might a little child, never to be cold again; he felt her full heart throb passionately against his own; he took from her burning lips the first pure, womanly kiss: she was all his.
10077{_}1384{_}But when she turned her head, there was a quick upward glance of her eyes, he knew not whether of appeal or thanks.
10077{_}1385{_}There was a Something in the world more near and real to her than he; he loved her the better for it: yet until he found that Unknown God, they were not one.
10077{_}1386{_}It was an uncertain step broke the silence, cracking the crusted snow.
10077{_}1387{_}"Why, Gaunt!"
10077{_}1388{_}said Palmer, "what are you doing in the cold?
10077{_}1389{_}Come to the fire, boy!"
10077{_}1390{_}He could afford to speak cordially, heartily, out of the great warmth in big own breast.
10077{_}1391{_}Theodora was heaping shavings on the ashes.
10077{_}1392{_}Gaunt took them from her.
10077{_}1393{_}"Let me do it," he muttered.
10077{_}1394{_}"I'd like to make your whole life warm, Dode,--your life, and--any one's you love."
10077{_}1395{_}Dode's face flushed with a happy smile.
10077{_}1396{_}Even David never would think of her as alone again.
10077{_}1397{_}Poor David!
10077{_}1398{_}She never before had thought how guileless he was,--how pitiful and solitary his life.
10077{_}1399{_}"Come home with us," she said, eagerly, holding out her hand.
10077{_}1400{_}He drew back, wiping the sweat from his face.
10077{_}1401{_}"You cannot see what is on my hand.
10077{_}1402{_}I can't touch you, Dode.

10077{_}1404{_}Let me alone."

10077{_}1406{_}"You stay here at the risk of your life.
10077{_}1407{_}Come to the house.
10077{_}1408{_}Theodora can hide us; and if they discover us, we can protect her together."
10077{_}1409{_}Gaunt smiled faintly.
10077{_}1410{_}"I must make my way to Springfield to-morrow.
10077{_}1411{_}My work is there,--my new work, Palmer."
10077{_}1412{_}Palmer looked troubled.
10077{_}1413{_}"I wish you had not taken it up.
10077{_}1414{_}This war may be needed to conquer a way for the day of peace and good-will among men; but you, who profess to be a seer and actor in that day, have only one work: to make it real to us now on earth, as your Master did, in the old time."
10077{_}1415{_}Gaunt did not speak,--fumbled among the chips at the fire.
10077{_}1416{_}He raised himself at last.
10077{_}1417{_}"I'm trying to do what's right," he said, in a subdued voice.
10077{_}1418{_}"I haven't had a pleasant life,--but it will come right at last, maybe."
10077{_}1419{_}"It will come right, David!"
10077{_}1420{_}said the girl.
10077{_}1421{_}His face lighted: her cheery voice sounded like a welcome ringing through his future years.
10077{_}1422{_}It was a good omen, coming from her whom he had wronged.
10077{_}1423{_}"Are you going now, Gaunt?"
10077{_}1424{_}asked Palmer, seeing him button his thin coat.
10077{_}1425{_}"Take my blanket,--nay, you shall.
10077{_}1426{_}As soon as I am strong enough, I'll find you at Springfield."
10077{_}1427{_}He wished he could hearten the poor unnerved soul, somehow.
10077{_}1428{_}Gaunt stopped outside, looking at them,--some uncertain thought coming and going in his face.
10077{_}1429{_}"I'll speak it out, whatever you may think.
10077{_}1430{_}Dode, I've done you a deadly hurt.
10077{_}1431{_}Don't ask me what it is,--God knows.
10077{_}1432{_}I'd like, before I go, to show you I love you in a pure, honorable way, you and your husband"----  The words choked in his throat; he stopped abruptly.
10077{_}1433{_}"Whatever you do, it will be honorable, David," said Palmer, gently.
10077{_}1434{_}"I think--God might take it as expiation,"--holding his hand to his head.
10077{_}1435{_}He did not speak again for a little while, then he said,----  "I will never see these old Virginian hills again.
10077{_}1436{_}I am going West; they will let me nurse in one of the hospitals;--that will be better than this that is on my hand."
10077{_}1437{_}Whatever intolerable pain lay in these words, he smothered it down, kept his voice steady.
10077{_}1438{_}"Do you understand, Douglas Palmer?
10077{_}1439{_}I will never see you again.
10077{_}1440{_}Nor Dode.
10077{_}1441{_}You love this woman; so did I,--as well as you.
10077{_}1442{_}Let me make her your wife before I go,--here, under this sky, with God looking down on us.
10077{_}1443{_}Will you?
10077{_}1444{_}I shall be happier to know that I have done it."
10077{_}1445{_}He waited while Douglas spoke eagerly to the girl, and then said,----  "Theodora, for God's sake don't refuse!
10077{_}1446{_}I have hurt you,--the marks of it you and I will carry to the grave.
10077{_}1447{_}Let me think you forgive me before I go.
10077{_}1448{_}Grant me this one request."
10077{_}1449{_}Did she guess the hurt he had done her?
10077{_}1450{_}Through all her fright and blushes, the woman in her spoke out nobly.
10077{_}1451{_}"I do not wish to know how you have wronged me.
10077{_}1452{_}Whatever it be, it was innocently done.
10077{_}1453{_}God will forgive you, and I do.
10077{_}1454{_}There shall be peace between us, David."
10077{_}1455{_}But she did not offer to touch his hand again: stood there, white and trembling.
10077{_}1456{_}"It shall be as you say," said Palmer.
10077{_}1457{_}So they were married, Douglas and Dode, in the wide winter night.
10077{_}1458{_}A few short words, that struck the very depths of their being, to make them one: simple words, wrung out of the man's thin lips with what suffering only he knew.
10077{_}1459{_}"Those whom God hath joined together let no man put asunder."
10077{_}1460{_}Thus he shut himself out from her forever.
10077{_}1461{_}But the prayer for a blessing on them came from as pure a heart as any child's that lives.
10077{_}1462{_}He bade them good-bye, cheerfully, when he had finished, and turned away, but came back presently, and said good-night again, looking in their faces steadily, then took his solitary way across the hills.
10077{_}1463{_}They never saw him again.
10077{_}1464{_}Bone, who had secured two horses by love or money or--confiscation, had stood mutely in the background, gulping down his opinion of this extraordinary scene.
10077{_}1465{_}He did not offer it now, only suggested it was "high time to be movin'," and when he was left alone, trudging through the snow, contented himself with smoothing his felt hat, and a breathless, "Ef dis nigger on'y knew what Mist' Perrine _would_ say!"
10077{_}1466{_}A June day.
10077{_}1467{_}These old Virginia hills have sucked in the winter's ice and snow, and throbbed it out again for the blue heaven to see in a whole summer's wealth of trees quivering with the luxury of being, in wreathed mosses, and bedded fern: the very blood that fell on them speaks in fair, grateful flowers to Him who doeth all things well.
10077{_}1468{_}Some healthy hearts, like the hills, you know, accept pain, and utter it again in fresher-blooded peace and life and love.
10077{_}1469{_}The evening sunshine lingers on Dode's little house to-day; the brown walls have the same cheery whim in life as the soul of their mistress, and catch the last ray of light,--will not let it go.
10077{_}1470{_}Bone, smoking his pipe at the garden-gate, looks at the house with drowsy complacency.
10077{_}1471{_}He calls it all "Mist' Dode's snuggery," now: he does not know that the rich, full-toned vigor of her happiness is the germ of all this life and beauty.
10077{_}1472{_}But he does know that the sun never seemed so warm, the air so pure, as this summer,--that about the quiet farm and homestead there is a genial atmosphere of peace: the wounded soldiers who come there often to be cured grow strong and calm in it; the war seems far-off to them; they have come somehow a step nearer the inner heaven.
10077{_}1473{_}Bone rejoices in showing off the wonders of the place to them, in matching Coly's shiny sides against the "Government beastesses," in talking of the giant red beets, or crumpled green cauliflower, breaking the rich garden-mould.
10077{_}1474{_}"Yer've no sich cherries nor taters nor raspberries as dem in de Norf, I'll bet!"
10077{_}1475{_}Even the crimson trumpet-flower on the wall is "a _Virginny_ creeper, Sah!"
10077{_}1476{_}But Bone learns something from them in exchange.
10077{_}1477{_}He does not boast so often now of being "ole Mars' Joe's man,"--sits and thinks profoundly, till he goes to sleep.
10077{_}1478{_}"Not of leavin' yer, Mist' Dode, I know what free darkies is, up dar; but dar's somefin' in a fellah's 'longin' ter hisself, af'er all!"
10077{_}1479{_}Dode only smiles at his deep cogitations, as he weeds the garden-beds, or fodders the stock.
10077{_}1480{_}She is a half-Abolitionist herself, and then she knows her State will soon be free.
10077{_}1481{_}So Dode, with deeper-lit eyes, and fresher rose in her cheek, stands in the door this summer evening waiting for her husband.
10077{_}1482{_}She cannot see him often; he has yet the work to do which he calls just and holy.
10077{_}1483{_}But he is coming now.
10077{_}1484{_}It is very quiet; she can hear her own heart beat slow and full; the warm air holds moveless the delicate scent of the clover; the bees hum her a drowsy good-night, as they pass; the locusts in the lindens have just begun to sing themselves to sleep; but the glowless crimson in the West holds her thought the longest.
10077{_}1485{_}She loves, understands color: it speaks to her of the Day waiting just behind this.
10077{_}1486{_}Her eyes fill with tears, she knows not why: her life seems rounded, complete, wrapt in a great peace; the grave at Manassas, and that planted with moss on the hill yonder, are in it; they only make her joy in living more tender and holy.
10077{_}1487{_}He has come now; stops to look at his wife's face, as though its fairness and meaning were new to him always.
10077{_}1488{_}There is no look in her eyes he loves so well to see as that which tells her Master is near her.
10077{_}1489{_}Sometimes she thinks he too----But she knows that "according to her faith it shall be unto her."
10077{_}1490{_}They are alone to-night; even Bone is asleep.
10077{_}1491{_}But in the midst of a crowd, they who love each other are alone together: as the first man and woman stood face to face in the great silent world, with God looking down, and only their love between them.
10077{_}1492{_}The same June evening lights the windows of a Western hospital.
10077{_}1493{_}There is not a fresh meadow-scented breath it gives that does not bring to some sick brain a thought of home, in a New-England village, or a Georgia rice-field.
10077{_}1494{_}The windows are open; the pure light creeping into poisoned rooms carries with it a Sabbath peace, they think.
10077{_}1495{_}One man stops in his hurried work, and looking out, grows cool in its tranquil calm.
10077{_}1496{_}So the sun used to set in old Virginia, he thinks.
10077{_}1497{_}A tall, slab-sided man, in the dress of a hospital-nurse: a worn face, but quick, sensitive; the patients like it better than any other: it looks as if the man had buried great pain in his life, and come now into its Indian-summer days.
10077{_}1498{_}The eyes are childish, eager, ready to laugh as cry,--the voice warm, chordant,--the touch of the hand unutterably tender.
10077{_}1499{_}A busy life, not one moment idle; but the man grows strong in it,--a healthy servant, doing a healthy work.
10077{_}1500{_}The patients are glad when he comes to their ward in turn.
10077{_}1501{_}How the windows open, and the fresh air comes in!
10077{_}1502{_}how the lazy nurses find a masterful will over them!
10077{_}1503{_}how full of innermost life he is!
10077{_}1504{_}how real his God seems to him!
10077{_}1505{_}He looks from the window now, his thought having time to close upon himself.
10077{_}1506{_}He holds up his busy, solitary life to God, with a happy smile.
10077{_}1507{_}He goes back to that bitter past, shrinking; but he knows its meaning now.
10077{_}1508{_}As the warm evening wanes into coolness and gray, the one unspoken pain of his life comes back, and whitens his cheerful face.
10077{_}1509{_}There is blood on his hands.
10077{_}1510{_}He sees the old man's gray hairs blown again by the wind, sees him stagger and fall.
10077{_}1511{_}Gaunt covers his bony face with his hands, but he cannot shut it out.
10077{_}1512{_}Yet he is learning to look back on even that with healthy, hopeful eyes.
10077{_}1513{_}He reads over again each day the misspelled words in the Bible,--thinking that the old man's haggard face looks down on him with the old kindly, forgiving smile.
10077{_}1514{_}What if his blood be on his hands?
10077{_}1515{_}He looks up now through the gathering night, into the land where spirits wait for us, as one who meets a friend's face, saying,--  "Let it be true what you have writ,--'The _Lord_ be between me and thee,' forever!"
10077{_}1516{_}EUPHORION.
10077{_}1517{_}"I will not longer       Earth-bound linger:       Loosen your hold on       Hand and on ringlet.
10077{_}1518{_}Girdle and garment;       Leave them: they're mine!"
10077{_}1519{_}"Bethink thee, bethink thee       To whom thou belongest!
10077{_}1520{_}Say, wouldst thou wound us,       Rudely destroying       Threefold the beauty,--       Mine, his, and thine?"
10077{_}1521{_}FAUST,--SECOND PART.
10077{_}1522{_}Nay, fold your arms, beloved Friends,     Above the hearts that vainly beat!
10077{_}1523{_}Or catch the rainbow where it bends,     And find your darling at its feet;    Or fix the fountain's varying shape,     The sunset-cloud's elusive dye,   The speech of winds that round the cape     Make music to the sea and sky:    So may you summon from the air     The loveliness that vanished hence,   And Twilight give his beauteous hair,     And Morning give his countenance,    And Life about his being clasp     Her rosy girdle once again:--   But no!
10077{_}1524{_}let go your stubborn grasp     On some wild hope, and take your pain!
10077{_}1525{_}For, through the crystal of your tears,     His love and beauty fairer shine;   The shadows of advancing years     Draw back, and leave him all divine.
10077{_}1526{_}And Death, that took him, cannot claim     The smallest vesture of his birth,--   The little life, a dancing flame     That hovered o'er the hills of earth,--    The finer soul, that unto ours     A subtle perfume seemed to be,   Like incense blown from April flowers     Beside the scarred and stormy tree,--    The wondering eyes, that ever saw     Some fleeting mystery in the air,   And felt the stars of evening draw     His heart to silence, childhood's prayer!
10077{_}1527{_}Our suns were all too fierce for him;     Our rude winds pierced him through and through;   But Heaven has valleys cool and dim,     And boscage sweet with starry dew.
10077{_}1528{_}There knowledge breathes in balmy air,     Not wrung, as here, with panting breast:   The wisdom born of toil you share;     But he, the wisdom born of rest.
10077{_}1529{_}For every picture here that slept,     A living canvas is unrolled;   The silent harp he might have swept     Leans to his touch its strings of gold.
10077{_}1530{_}Believe, dear Friends, they murmur still     Some sweet accord to those you play,   That happier winds of Eden thrill     With echoes of the earthly lay;    That he, for every triumph won,     Whereto your poet-souls aspire,   Sees opening, in that perfect sun,     Another blossom's bud of fire!
10077{_}1531{_}Each song, of Love and Sorrow born,     Another flower to crown your boy,--   Each shadow here his ray of morn,     Till Grief shall clasp the hand of Joy!
10077{_}1532{_}HOUSE-BUILDING.
10077{_}1533{_}Because our architecture is bad, and because the architecture of our forefathers in the Middle Ages was good, Mr. Ruskin and others seem to think there is no salvation for us until we build in the same spirit as they did.
10077{_}1534{_}But that we should do so no more follows than that we should envy those geological ages when the club-mosses were of the size of forest-trees, and the frogs as big as oxen.
10077{_}1535{_}There are many advantages to be had in the forests of the Amazon and the interior of Borneo,--inexhaustible fertility, endless water-power,--but no one thinks of going there to live.
10077{_}1536{_}No age is without its attractions.
10077{_}1537{_}There would be much to envy in the Greek or the Roman life, if we could have them clear of drawbacks.
10077{_}1538{_}Many persons would be glad always to find Emerson in State Street, or sauntering in the Mall, ready to talk with all comers,--or to hear the latest words of Bancroft or Lowell from their own lips at the cattle-show or the militia-muster.
10077{_}1539{_}The Roman villas had some excellent features,--the peristyle of statues, the cryptoporticus with its midnight coolness and shade of a July noon, the mosaic floor, and the glimmering frescoes of the ceiling.
10077{_}1540{_}But we are content to get our poets and historians in their books, and to take the pine-grove for our noonday walk, or to wait till night has transformed the street into a cryptoporticus nobler than Titus's.
10077{_}1541{_}It is as history that these things charm us; but the charm vanishes, when, even in fancy, we bring them into contact with our actual lives.
10077{_}1542{_}So it is with the medieval architecture.
10077{_}1543{_}It is true, in studying these wonderful fossils, a regret for our present poverty, and a desire to appropriate something from the ancient riches, will at times come over us.
10077{_}1544{_}But this feeling, if it be more than slight and transient, if it seriously influence our conduct, is somewhat factitious or somewhat morbid.
10077{_}1545{_}Let us be a little disinterested in our admiration, and not, like children, cry for all we see.
10077{_}1546{_}We have our share: let us leave the dead theirs.
10077{_}1547{_}The fallacy lies in the supposition, that, besides all their advantages, they had all ours too.
10077{_}1548{_}It is with our mental as with our bodily vision,--we see only what is remote; and the image to the mind depends, not only upon seeing, but upon _not seeing_.
10077{_}1549{_}In the distant star, all foulness and gloom are lost, and only the pure splendor reaches us.
10077{_}1550{_}Inspired by Mr. Ruskin's eloquence, the neophyte sets forth with contrition to put his precepts into practice.
10077{_}1551{_}But the counterstatement which he had overlooked does not, therefore, cease to exist.
10077{_}1552{_}At the outset, he finds unexpected sacrifices are demanded.
10077{_}1553{_}And, as money is the common measure of the forces disposable, the hindrances take the form of increase of cost.
10077{_}1554{_}Before the first step can be taken towards doing anything as Mr. Ruskin would have it done, he discovers that at least it will cost enormously more to do it in that way.
10077{_}1555{_}The lamps of truth and sacrifice demand such expensive nourishment, that he is forced to ask himself whether they are of themselves really sufficient to live by.
10077{_}1556{_}It is not that we are poorer or more penurious than our ancestors, but that we have more wants than they, and that the new wants overshadow the old.
10077{_}1557{_}What is spent in one direction must be spared in another.
10077{_}1558{_}The matter-of-course necessaries of our life were luxuries or were unknown to them.
10077{_}1559{_}First of all, the luxury of freedom,--political, social, and domestic,--with the habits it creates, is the source of great and ever-increasing expense.
10077{_}1560{_}We are still much behindhand in this matter, and shall by-and-by spend more largely upon it.
10077{_}1561{_}But, compared with our ancestors, individual culture, to which freedom is the means, absorbs a large share of our expenditure.
10077{_}1562{_}The noble architecture of the thirteenth century was the work of corporations, of a society that knew only corporations, and where individual culture was a crime.
10077{_}1563{_}Dante had made the discovery that it is the man that creates his own position, not the accident of birth.
10077{_}1564{_}But his life shows how this belief isolated him.
10077{_}1565{_}Nor was the coincidence between the artistic spirit of the age and its limitations accidental.
10077{_}1566{_}Just in proportion as the spirit of individualism penetrated society, and began to show itself as the Renaissance, architecture declined.
10077{_}1567{_}The Egyptian pyramids are marvels to us, because we are accustomed to look upon the laborer as a man.
10077{_}1568{_}But once allow that he is only so much brute force,--cheap, readily available, and to be had in endless supply, but as a moral entity less to be respected than a cat or a heron, and the marvel ceases.
10077{_}1569{_}Should not the building be great to which man himself is sacrificed?
10077{_}1570{_}Later, the builders are no longer slaves; but man is still subordinate to his own work, adores the work of his hands.
10077{_}1571{_}This stands for him, undertakes to represent him, though, from its partial nature, it can only typify certain aspects or functions of him.
10077{_}1572{_}A Gothic cathedral is an attempt at a universal expression of humanity, a stone image of society, in which each particle, insignificant by itself, has its meaning in the connection.
10077{_}1573{_}It was the fresh interest in the attempt that gave birth to that wonderful architecture.
10077{_}1574{_}This is the interest it still has, but now only historical, since the discovery was made that the particle is greater than the mass,--that it is for the sake of the individual that society and its institutions exist.
10077{_}1575{_}Ever since, a process of disintegration has been going on, resulting in a progressive reversal of the previous relation.
10077{_}1576{_}Not the private virtues of the structure, but its uses, are now uppermost, and ever more and more developed.
10077{_}1577{_}Even in our own short annals something of this process may be traced.
10077{_}1578{_}Old gentlemen complain of the cost of our houses.
10077{_}1579{_}The houses of their boyhood, they say, were handsomer and better built, yet cost less.
10077{_}1580{_}There is some truth in this, for the race of architect-builders hardly reaches into this century.
10077{_}1581{_}But if the comparison be pushed into details, we soon come to the conviction that the owners of these houses were persons whose habits were, in many respects, uncouth and barbarous.
10077{_}1582{_}It is easy to provide in the lump; but with decency, privacy, independence,--in short, with a high degree of respect on the part of the members of the household for each other's individuality,--expense begins.
10077{_}1583{_}Letarouilly says it is difficult to discover in the Roman palaces of the Renaissance any reference to special uses of the different apartments.
10077{_}1584{_}It was to the outside, the vestibule, courtyard, and staircase, that care and study were given: the inside was intended only as a measure of the riches and importance of the owner, not as his habitation.
10077{_}1585{_}The part really inhabited by him was the _mezzanino_,--a low, intermediate story, where he and his family were kennelled out of the way.
10077{_}1586{_}Has any admiring traveller ever asked himself how he could establish himself, with wife and children, in the Foscari or the Vendramin palace?
10077{_}1587{_}To live in them, it would be necessary to build a house inside.
10077{_}1588{_}Nor is there any ground for saying that the fault is in the builders,--that the old builders met the demands of their time, and would equally satisfy the demands of our time, without sacrifice of their art.
10077{_}1589{_}The first demand in the days of good architecture was, that the building should have an independent artistic value beyond its use.
10077{_}1590{_}This is what architecture requires; for architecture is building, _pure_,--building for its own sake, not as means.
10077{_}1591{_}What Mr. Garbett says is, no doubt, quite true,--that nothing was ever made, for taste's sake, less efficient than it might have been.
10077{_}1592{_}But many things were made _more_ efficient than they might have been; or, rather, this is always the character of good architecture.
10077{_}1593{_}It is in this surplus of perfection, above bare necessity, that its claim to rank among the fine arts consists.
10077{_}1594{_}This character the builders of the good times, accordingly, never left out of sight; so that, if their means were limited, they lavished all upon one point,--made that overflow with riches, and left the rest plain and bare; never did they spread their pittance thin to cover the whole, as we do.
10077{_}1595{_}It is for this reason that so few of the great cathedrals were finished, and that in buildings of all kinds we so often find the decoration in patches, sharply marked off from the rest of the structure.
10077{_}1596{_}This noble profuseness is not, indeed, necessarily decoration; the essence of it is an independent value and interest in the building, aside from the temporary and accidental employment.
10077{_}1597{_}The spires and the flying-buttresses of the Northern cathedrals cannot be defended on the ground of thrifty construction.
10077{_}1598{_}The Italian churches accomplished that as well without either.
10077{_}1599{_}How remote the reference to use in the mighty portals of Rheims, or the soaring vaultings of Amiens and Beauvais!
10077{_}1600{_}Does anybody suppose that Michel Angelo, when he undertook to raise the dome of the Pantheon into the air, was thinking of the most economical way of roofing a given space?
10077{_}1601{_}These fine works have their whole value as expression; it is with their visible contempt of thrift that our admiration begins.
10077{_}1602{_}They pared away the stone to the minimum that safety demanded, and beyond it,--yet not from thrift, but to make the design more preeminent and necessary, and to owe as little as possible to the inert strength of the material.
10077{_}1603{_}But though we admire the result, we have grown out of sympathy with the cause, the state of mind that produced it, and so the root wherefrom the like should be produced is cut off.
10077{_}1604{_}There is no reason to suppose that the old builders were men of a different kind from ours, more earnest, more poetical.
10077{_}1605{_}The stories about the science of the medieval masons are rubbish.
10077{_}1606{_}All men are in earnest about something; our men are as good as they, and would have built as well, had they been born at the right time for it.
10077{_}1607{_}But now they are thinking of other things.
10077{_}1608{_}The Dilettanti Society sent Mr. Penrose to Athens to study in the ancient remains there the optical corrections which it was alleged the Greeks made in the horizontal lines of their buildings.
10077{_}1609{_}Mr. Penrose made careful measurements, establishing the fact, and a folio volume of plates was published to illustrate the discovery, and evince the unequalled nicety of the Greek eye.
10077{_}1610{_}But the main point, namely, that a horizontal line above the level of the eye, in order to appear horizontal, must bend slightly upwards, was pointed out to me years ago by a common plasterer.
10077{_}1611{_}It is not that our builders are degenerate, but that their art is a trade, occupies only their hands, not their minds, and this by no fault in them or in anybody, but by the natural progress of the world.
10077{_}1612{_}In each age by turn some one mental organ is in a state of hypertrophy; immediately that becomes the medium of expression,--not that it is the only possible or even the best, but that its time has come,--then it gives place to another.
10077{_}1613{_}Architecture is dead and gone to dust long ago.
10077{_}1614{_}We are not called upon to sing threnodies over it, still less to attempt to galvanize a semblance of life into it.
10077{_}1615{_}If we must blame somebody, let it not be the builder, but his employers, who, caring less even than he for the reality of good architecture, (for the material itself teaches him something,) force him into these puerilities in order to gratify their dissolute fancies.
10077{_}1616{_}If these views seem to any one low and prosaic, let me remind him that poetry does not differ from prose in being false.
10077{_}1617{_}We must respect the facts.
10077{_}1618{_}If there were in this country any considerable number of persons to whom the buildings they daily enter had any positive permanent value besides convenience,--who looked upon the church, the bank, or the house, as upon a poem or a statue,--the birth of a national architecture would be assured.
10077{_}1619{_}But as the fact stands, while utility, and that of a temporary and makeshift sort, is really the first consideration, we are not yet ready to acknowledge this to others or to ourselves, and so fail to get from it what negative advantage we might, but blunder on under some fancied necessity, spending what we can ill spare, to the defrauding of legitimate demands, as a sort of sin-offering for our aesthetic deficiency, or as a blind to conceal it.
10077{_}1620{_}The falsehood, like all falsehood, defeats itself; the pains we take only serve to make the failure more complete.
10077{_}1621{_}This is displayed most fully in the doings of "Building Committees."
10077{_}1622{_}Here we see what each member (perhaps it would be more just to say the least judicious among them) would do in his own case, were he free from the rude admonitions of necessity.
10077{_}1623{_}He has at least to live in his own house, and so cannot escape some attention to the substantial requirements of it; though some houses, too, seem emancipated from such considerations, and to have been built for any end rather than to live in.
10077{_}1624{_}But in catering for the public, it is the _outsiders_ alone that seem to be consulted, the careless passer-by, who for once will pause a moment to commend or to sneer at the facade,--not the persons whose lives for years, perhaps, are to be affected by the internal arrangement.
10077{_}1625{_}It is doubtless from a suspicion, more or less obscure, of the incoherency of their purpose, that such committees usually fall into the hands of a "practical man,"--that is, a man impassive to principles, of hardihood or bluntness of perception enough to carry into effect their vague fancies, and spare them from coming face to face with their inconsistencies.
10077{_}1626{_}Thus fairly adrift and kept adrift from the main purpose, there is no vagary impossible to them,--churches in which there is no hearing, hospitals contrived to develop disease, museums of tinder, libraries impossible to light or warm.
10077{_}1627{_}And what gain comes to beauty from these sacrifices, let our streets answer.
10077{_}1628{_}Good architecture requires before all things a definite aim, long persisted in.
10077{_}1629{_}It never was an invention, anywhere, but always a gradual growth.
10077{_}1630{_}What chance of that here?
10077{_}1631{_}The only chance clearly is to cut away till we come to the solid ground of real, not fancied, requirement.
10077{_}1632{_}As long as it is our whims, and not our necessities, that build, it matters little how much pains we take, how learned and assiduous we are.
10077{_}1633{_}I have no hope of any considerable advantage from the abundant exhortation to frankness and genuineness in the use of materials, unless it lead first of all to a more frank and genuine consideration of the occasion for using the materials at all.
