10077{_}2923{_}Of the remainder, 5,169, who were subjects of further inquiry, 2,443 were rejected for reasons connected with their physical or mental condition:--             243 mal-formed,           630 unsound in physical constitution,            16 unsound in mind,           314 had diseased eyes,            55 had diseased ears,           314 had hernia,         1,071 had varicose veins,         ------- Total,  2,443   Only 2,726 were accepted, being 52.7 per cent, of those who were examined, and less than 17 per cent., or about one-sixth, of all who offered themselves as candidates for the army, in that year.
10077{_}2924{_}[4]  In time of peace, the character of the men who desire to become soldiers differs with the degree of public prosperity.
10077{_}2925{_}When business is good, most men obtain employment in the more desirable and profitable avocations of civil life.
10077{_}2926{_}Then a larger proportion of those who are willing to enter the army are unfitted, by their habits or their health, for the occupations of peace, and go to the rendezvous only as a last resort, to obtain their bread.
10077{_}2927{_}But when business falters, a larger and a better class are thrown out of work, and are glad to enter the service of the country by bearing arms.
10077{_}2928{_}The year 1852 was one of prosperity, and affords, therefore, no indication of the class and character of men who are willing to enlist in the average years.
10077{_}2929{_}The Government Reports state that in some other years 6,383 were accepted and 3,617 rejected out of 10,000 that offered to enlist.
10077{_}2930{_}But in time of war, when the country is endangered, and men have a higher motive for entering its service than mere employment and wages, those of a better class both as to character and health flock to the army; and in the present war, the army is composed, in great degree, of men of the highest personal character and social position, who leave the most desirable and lucrative employments to serve their country as soldiers.
10077{_}2931{_}As, then, the army excludes, or intends to exclude, from its ranks all the defective, weak, and sick, it begins with a much higher average of health and vigor, a greater power of action, of endurance, and of resisting the causes of disease, than the mass of men of the same ages in civil life.
10077{_}2932{_}It is composed of men in the fulness of strength and efficiency.
10077{_}2933{_}This is the vital machinery with which Governments propose to do their martial work; and the amount of vital force which belongs to these living machines, severally and collectively, is the capital with which they intend to accomplish their purposes.
10077{_}2934{_}Every wise Government begins the business of war with a good capital of life, a large quantity of vital force in its army.
10077{_}2935{_}So far they do well; but more is necessary.
10077{_}2936{_}This complete and fitting preparation alone is not sufficient to carry on the martial process through weeks and months of labor and privation.
10077{_}2937{_}Not only must the living machinery of bone and flesh be well selected, but its force must be sustained, it must be kept in the most effective condition and in the best and most available working order.
10077{_}2938{_}For this there are two established conditions, that admit of no variation nor neglect: first, a sufficient supply of suitable nutriment, and faithful regard to all the laws of health; and, second, the due appropriation of the vital force that is thus from day to day created.
10077{_}2939{_}A due supply of appropriate food and of pure air, sufficient protection and cleansing of the surface, moderate labor and refreshing rest, are the necessary conditions of health, and cannot be disregarded, in the least degree, without a loss of force.
10077{_}2940{_}The privation of even a single meal, or the use of food that is hard of digestion or innutritious, and the loss of any of the needful sleep, are followed by a corresponding loss of effective power, as surely as the slackened fire in the furnace is followed by lessened steam and power in the engine.
10077{_}2941{_}Whosoever, then, wishes to sustain his own forces or those of his laborers with the least cost, and use them with the greatest effect, must take Nature on her own terms.
10077{_}2942{_}It is vain to try to evade or alter her conditions.
10077{_}2943{_}The Kingdom of Heaven is not divided against itself.
10077{_}2944{_}It makes no compromises, not even for the necessities of nations.
10077{_}2945{_}It will not consent that any one, even the least, of its laws shall be set aside, to advance any other, however important.
10077{_}2946{_}Each single law stands by itself, and exacts complete obedience to its own requirements: it gives its own rewards and inflicts its own punishments.
10077{_}2947{_}The stomach will not digest tough and hard or old salted meats, or heavy bread, without demanding and receiving a great and perhaps an almost exhausting proportion of the nervous energies.
10077{_}2948{_}The nutritive organs will not create vigorous muscles and effective limbs, unless the blood is constantly and appropriately recruited.
10077{_}2949{_}The lungs will not decarbonize and purify the blood with foul air, that has been breathed over and over and lost its oxygen.
10077{_}2950{_}However noble or holy the purpose for which human power is to be used, it will not be created, except according to the established conditions.
10077{_}2951{_}The strength of the warrior in battle cannot be sustained, except in the appointed way, even though the fate of all humanity depend on his exertions.
10077{_}2952{_}Nature keeps an exact account with all her children, and gives power in proportion to their fulfilment of her conditions.
10077{_}2953{_}She measures out and sustains vital force according to the kind and fitness of the raw material provided for her.
10077{_}2954{_}When we deal liberally with her, she deals liberally with us.
10077{_}2955{_}For everything we give to her she makes a just return.
10077{_}2956{_}The stomach, the nutrient arteries, the lungs, have no love, no patriotism, no pity; but they are perfectly honest.
10077{_}2957{_}The healthy digestive organs will extract and pay over to the blood-vessels just so much of the nutritive elements as the food we eat contains in an extractible form, and no more; and for this purpose they will demand and take just so much of the nervous energy as may be needed.
10077{_}2958{_}The nutrient arteries will convert into living flesh just so much of the nutritive elements as the digestive organs give them, and no more.
10077{_}2959{_}The lungs will send out from the body as many of the atoms of exhausted and dead flesh as the oxygen we give them will convert into carbonic acid and water, and this is all they can do.
10077{_}2960{_}In these matters, the vital organs are as honest and as faithful as the boiler, that gives forth steam in the exact ratio of the heat which the burning fuel evolves and the fitness of the water that is supplied to it; and neither can be persuaded to do otherwise.
10077{_}2961{_}The living machine of bone and flesh and the dead machine of iron prepare their forces according to the means they have, not according to the ulterior purpose to which those forces are to be applied.
10077{_}2962{_}They do this alike for all.
10077{_}2963{_}They do it as well for the sinner as for the saint,--as well for the traitorous Secessionist striving to destroy his country as for the patriot endeavoring to sustain it.
10077{_}2964{_}In neither case is it a matter of will, but of necessity.
10077{_}2965{_}The amount of power to be generated in both living and dead machines is simply a question of quality and quantity of provision for the purpose.
10077{_}2966{_}So much food, air, protection given produce so much strength.
10077{_}2967{_}A proposition to reduce the amount of either of these necessarily involves the proposition to reduce the available force.
10077{_}2968{_}Whoever determines to eat or give his men less or poorer food, or impure air, practically determines to do less work.
10077{_}2969{_}In all this management of the human body, we are sure to get what we pay for, and we are equally sure not to get what we do not pay for.
10077{_}2970{_}All Governments have tried, and are now, in various degrees, trying, the experiment of privation in their armies.
10077{_}2971{_}The soldier cannot carry with him the usual means and comforts of home.
10077{_}2972{_}He must give these up the moment he enters the martial ranks, and reduce his apparatus of living to the smallest possible quantity.
10077{_}2973{_}He must generally limit himself to a portable house, kitchen, cooking-apparatus, and wardrobe, and to an entire privation of furniture, and sometimes submit to a complete destitution of everything except the provision he may carry in his haversack and the blanket he can carry on his back.
10077{_}2974{_}When stationary, he commonly sleeps in barracks; but he spends most of his time in the field and sleeps in tents.
10077{_}2975{_}Occasionally he is compelled to sleep in the open air, without any covering but his blanket, and to cook in an extemporized kitchen, which he may make of a few stones piled together or of a hole in the earth, with only a kettle, that he carries on his back, for cooking-apparatus.
10077{_}2976{_}In all cases and conditions, whether in fort or in field, in barrack, tent, or open air, he is limited to the smallest artificial habitation, the least amount of furniture and conveniences, the cheapest and most compact food, and the rudest cookery.
10077{_}2977{_}He is, therefore, never so well protected against the elements, nor, when sleeping under cover, so well supplied with air for respiration, as he is at home.
10077{_}2978{_}Moreover, when lodging abroad, he cannot take his choice of places; he is liable, from the necessities of war, to encamp in wet and malarious spots, and to be exposed to chills and miasms of unhealthy districts.
10077{_}2979{_}He is necessarily exposed to weather of every kind,--to cold, to rains, to storms; and when wet, he has not the means of warming himself, nor of drying or changing his clothing.
10077{_}2980{_}His life, though under martial discipline, is irregular.
10077{_}2981{_}At times, he has to undergo severe and protracted labors, forced marches, and the violent and long-continued struggles of combat; at other times, he has not exercise sufficient for health.
10077{_}2982{_}His food is irregularly served.
10077{_}2983{_}He is sometimes short of provisions, and compelled to pass whole days in abstinence or on shore allowance.
10077{_}2984{_}Occasionally he cannot obtain even water to drink, through hours of thirsty toil.
10077{_}2985{_}No Government nor managers of war have ever yet been able to make exact and unfailing provision for the wants and necessities of their armies, as men usually do for themselves and their families at home.
10077{_}2986{_}SUPPOSED DANGERS TO THE SOLDIER.
10077{_}2987{_}From the earliest recorded periods of the world, men have gone forth to war, for the purpose of destroying or overcoming their enemies, and with the chance of being themselves destroyed or overthrown.
10077{_}2988{_}Public authorities have generally taken account of the number of their own men who have been wounded and killed in battle, and of the casualties in the opposing armies.
10077{_}2989{_}Gunpowder and steel, and the manifold weapons, instruments, and means of destruction in the hands of the enemy are commonly considered as the principal, if not the only sources of danger to the soldier, and ground of anxiety to his friends; and the nation reckons its losses in war by the number of those who were wounded and killed in battle.
10077{_}2990{_}But the suffering and waste of life, apart from the combat, the sickness, the depreciation of vital force, the withering of constitutional energy, and the mortality in camp and fortress, in barrack, tent, and hospital, have not usually been the subjects of such careful observation, nor the grounds of fear to the soldier and of anxiety to those who are interested in his safety.
10077{_}2991{_}Consequently, until within the present century, comparatively little attention has been given to the dangers that hang over the army out of the battle-field, and but little provision has been made, by the combatants or their rulers, to obviate or relieve them.
10077{_}2992{_}No Government in former times, and few in later years, have taken and published complete accounts of the diseases of their armies, and of the deaths that followed in consequence.
10077{_}2993{_}Some such records have been made and printed, but these are mostly fragmentary and partial, and on the authority of individuals, officers, surgeons, scholars, and philanthropists.
10077{_}2994{_}It must not be forgotten that the army is originally composed of picked men, while the general community includes not only the imperfect, diseased, and weak that belong to itself, but also those who are rejected from the army.
10077{_}2995{_}If, then, the conditions, circumstances, and habits of both were equally favorable, there would be less sickness and a lower rate of mortality among the soldiers than among men of the same ages at home.
10077{_}2996{_}But if in the army there should be found more sickness and death than in the community at home, or even an equal amount, it is manifestly chargeable to the presence of more deteriorating and destructive influences in the military than in civil life.
10077{_}2997{_}SICKNESS AND MORTALITY IN CIVIL LIFE.
10077{_}2998{_}The amount of sickness among the people at home is not generally recognized, still less is it carefully measured and recorded.
10077{_}2999{_}But the experience and calculations of the Friendly Societies of Great Britain, and of other associations for Health-Assurance there and elsewhere, afford sufficient data for determining the proportion of time lost in sickness by men of various ages.
10077{_}3000{_}These Friendly Societies are composed mainly of men of the working-classes, from which most of the soldiers of the British army are drawn.
10077{_}3001{_}According to the calculations and tables of Mr. Ansel, in his work on "Friendly Societies," the men of the army-ages, from 20 to 40, in the working-classes, lose, on an average, five days and six-tenths of a day by sickness in each year, which will make one and a half per cent, of the males of this age and class constantly sick.
10077{_}3002{_}Mr. Neison's calculations and tables, in his "Contributions to Vital Statistics," make this average somewhat over seven days' yearly sickness, and one and ninety-two hundredths of one per cent, constantly sick.
10077{_}3003{_}These were the bases of the rates adopted by the Health-Assurance companies in New England, and their experience shows that the amount of sickness in these Northern States is about the same as, if not somewhat greater than, that in Great Britain, among any definite number of men.
10077{_}3004{_}The rate of mortality is more easily ascertained, and is generally calculated and determined in civilized nations.
10077{_}3005{_}This rate, among all classes of males, between 20 and 40 years old, in England and Wales, is .92 per cent.
10077{_}3006{_}: that is, 92 will die out of 10,000 men of these ages, on an average, in each year; but in the healthiest districts the rate is only 77 in 10,000.
10077{_}3007{_}The mortality among the males of Massachusetts, of the same ages, according to Mr. Elliott's calculations, is 1.11 per cent, or 111 in 10,000.
10077{_}3008{_}This maybe safely assumed as the rate of mortality in all New England.
10077{_}3009{_}That of the Southern States is somewhat greater.
10077{_}3010{_}These rates of sickness and death--one and a half or one and ninety-two hundredths per cent, constantly sick, and seventy-seven to one hundred and eleven dying, in each year, among ten thousand living--may be considered as the proportion of males, of the army-ages, that should be constantly taken away from active labor and business by illness, and that should be annually lost by death.
10077{_}3011{_}Whether at home, amidst the usually favorable circumstances and the average comforts, or in the army, under privation and exposure, men of these ages may be presumed to be necessarily subject to this amount, at least, of loss of vital force and life.
10077{_}3012{_}And these rates may be adopted as the standard of comparison of the sanitary influences of civil and military life.
10077{_}3013{_}SICKNESS AND MORTALITY OF THE ARMY IN PEACE.
10077{_}3014{_}Soldiers are subject to different influences and exposures, and their waste and loss of life differ, in peace and war.
10077{_}3015{_}In peace they are mostly stationary, at posts, forts, and in cantonments.
10077{_}3016{_}They generally live in barracks, with fixed habits and sufficient means of subsistence.
10077{_}3017{_}They have their regular supplies of food and clothing and labor, and are protected from the elements, heat, cold, and storms.
10077{_}3018{_}They are seldom or never subjected to privation or excessive fatigue.
10077{_}3019{_}But in war they are in the field, and sleep in tents which are generally too full and often densely crowded.
10077{_}3020{_}Sometimes they sleep in huts, and occasionally in the open air.
10077{_}3021{_}They are liable to exposures, hardships, and privations, to uncertain supplies of food and bad cookery.
10077{_}3022{_}The report of the commission appointed by the British Government to inquire into the sanitary condition, of the army shows a remarkable and unexpected degree of mortality among the troops stationed at home under the most favorable circumstances, as well as among those abroad.
10077{_}3023{_}The Foot-Guards are the very _elite_ of the whole army; they are the most perfect of the faultless in form and in health.
10077{_}3024{_}They are the pets of the Government and the people.
10077{_}3025{_}They are stationed at London and Windsor, and lodged in magnificent barracks, apparently ample for their accommodation.
10077{_}3026{_}They are clothed and fed with extraordinary care, and are supposed to have every means of health.
10077{_}3027{_}And yet their record shows a sad difference between their rate of mortality and that of men of the same ages in civil life.
10077{_}3028{_}A similar excess of mortality was found to exist among all the home-army, which includes many thousand soldiers, stationed in various towns and places throughout the kingdom.
10077{_}3029{_}The following table exhibits the annual mortality in these classes.
10077{_}3030{_}[5]   DEATHS IN 10,000.
10077{_}3031{_}Age       Civilians   Foot-Guards   Home-Army  20 to 25      84          216          170 25 to 30      92          211          183 30 to 35     102          195          184 35 to 40     116          224          193   Through the fifteen years from 1839 to 1853 inclusive, the annual mortality of all the army, excepting the artillery, engineers, and West India and colonial corps, was 330 among 10,000 living; while that among the same number of males of the army-ages, in all England and Wales, was 92, and in the healthiest districts only 77.
10077{_}3032{_}[6]  There is no official account at hand of the general mortality in the Russian army on the peace-establishment; yet, according to Boudin, in one portion, consisting of 192,834 men, 144,352 had been sick, and 7,541, or 38 per 1,000, died in one year.
10077{_}3033{_}[7]  The Prussian army, with an average of 150,582 men, lost by death, during the ten years 1829 to 1838, 1,975 in each year, which is at the rate of 13 per 1,000 living.
10077{_}3034{_}[8]  The mortality of the Piedmontese army, from 1834 to 1843 inclusive, was 158 in 10,000, while that of the males at home was 92 in the same number living.
10077{_}3035{_}From 1775 to 1791, seventeen years, the mortality among the cavalry was 181, and among the infantry 349, out of 10,000 living; but in the ten years from 1834 to 1843 these rates were only 108 and 215.
10077{_}3036{_}[9]  Colored troops are employed by the British Government in all their colonies and possessions in tropical climates.
10077{_}3037{_}The mortality of these soldiers is known, and also that of the colored male civilians in the East Indies and in the West-India Islands and South-American Provinces.
10077{_}3038{_}In four of these, the rate of mortality is higher among the male slaves than among the colored soldiers; but in all the others, this rate is higher in the army.
10077{_}3039{_}In all the West-Indian and South-American possessions of Great Britain, the average rate of deaths is 25 per cent, greater among the black troops than among the black males of all ages on the plantations and in the towns.
10077{_}3040{_}The soldiers are of the healthier ages, 20 to 40, but the civilians include both the young and the old: if these could be excluded, and the comparison made between soldiers and laborers of the same ages, the difference in favor of civil pursuits would appear much greater.
10077{_}3041{_}Throughout the world, where the armies of Great Britain are stationed or serve, the death-rate is greater among the troops than among civilians of the same races and ages, except among the colored troops in Tobago, Montserrat, Antigua, and Granada in America, and among the Sepoys in the East Indies.
10077{_}3042{_}[10]  In the army of the United States, during the period from 1840 to 1854, not including the two years of the Mexican War, there was an average of 9,278 men, or an aggregate of 120,622 years of service, equal to so many men serving one year.
10077{_}3043{_}Among these and during this period, there were 342,107 cases of sickness reported by the surgeons, and 3,416 deaths from disease, showing a rate of mortality of 2.83 per cent., or two and a half times as great as that among the males of Massachusetts of the army-ages, and three times as great as that in England and Wales.
10077{_}3044{_}The attacks of sickness average almost three for each man in each year.
10077{_}3045{_}This is manifestly more than that which falls upon men of these ages at home.
10077{_}3046{_}[11]  SICKNESS AND MORTALITY OF THE ARMY IN WAR.
10077{_}3047{_}Thus far the sickness and mortality of the army in time of peace only has been considered.
10077{_}3048{_}The experience of war tells a more painful story of the dangers of the men engaged in it.
10077{_}3049{_}Sir John Pringle states, that, in the British armies that were sent to the Low Countries and Germany, in the years 1743 to 1747, a great amount of sickness and mortality prevailed.
10077{_}3050{_}He says, that, besides those who were suffering from wounds, "at some periods more than one-fifth of the army were in the hospitals."
10077{_}3051{_}"One regiment had over one-half of its men sick."
10077{_}3052{_}"In July and August, 1743, one-half of the army had the dysentery."
10077{_}3053{_}"In 1747, four battalions," of 715 men each, "at South Beveland and Walcheren, both in field and in quarters, were so very sickly, that, at the height of the epidemic, some of these corps had but one hundred men fit for duty; six-sevenths of their numbers were sick.
10077{_}3054{_}"[12] "At the end of the campaign the Royal Battalion had but four men who had not been ill." And "when these corps went into winter-quarters, their sick, in proportion to their men fit for duty, were nearly as four to one.
10077{_}3055{_}"[13] In 1748, dysentery prevailed.
10077{_}3056{_}"In one regiment of 500 men, 150 were sick at the end of five weeks; 200 were sick after two months; and at the end of the campaign, they had in all but thirty who had never been ill." "In Johnson's regiment sometimes one-half were sick; and in the Scotch Fusileers 300 were ill at one time.
10077{_}3057{_}"[14]  The British army in Egypt, in 1801, had from 103 to 261 and an average of 182 sick in each thousand; and the French army had an average of 125 in 1,000, or one-eighth of the whole, on the sick-list.
10077{_}3058{_}[15]  In July, 1809, the British Government sent another army, of 39,219 men, to the Netherlands.
10077{_}3059{_}They were stationed at Walcheren, which was the principal seat of the sickness and suffering of their predecessors, sixty or seventy years before.
10077{_}3060{_}Fever and dysentery attacked this second army as they had the first, and with a similar virulence and destructiveness.
10077{_}3061{_}In two months after landing,   Sept. 13, 7,626 were on the sick-list.   "
10077{_}3062{_}19, 8,123     "    "   "   21, 8,684     "    "   "   23, 9,046     "    "   In ninety-seven days 12,867 were sent home sick; and on the 22d of October there were only 4,000 effective men left fit for duty out of this army of about 40,000 healthy men, who had left England within less than four months.
10077{_}3063{_}On the 1st of February of the next year, there were 11,513 on the sick-list, and 15,570 had been lost or disabled.
10077{_}3064{_}Between January 1st and June of the same year, (1810,) 36,500 were admitted to the hospitals, and 8,000, or more than 20 per cent., died, which is equal to an annual rate of 48 per cent, mortality.
10077{_}3065{_}The British army in Spain and Portugal suffered greatly through the Peninsular War, from 1808 to 1814.
10077{_}3066{_}During the whole of that period, there was a constant average of 209 per 1,000 on the sick-list, and the proportion was sometimes swelled to 330 per 1,000.
10077{_}3067{_}Through the forty-one months ending May 25th, 1814, with an average of 61,511 men, there was an average of 13,815 in the hospitals, which is 22.5 per cent.
10077{_}3068{_}; of these only one-fifteenth, or 1.5 per cent.
10077{_}3069{_}of the whole army, were laid up on account of injuries in battle, and 21 per cent.
10077{_}3070{_}were disabled by diseases.
10077{_}3071{_}From these causes 24,930 died, which is an annual average of 7,296, or a rate of 11.8 per cent, mortality.
10077{_}3072{_}[16]  No better authority can be adduced, for the condition of men engaged in the actual service of war, than Lord Wellington.
10077{_}3073{_}On the 14th of November, 1809, he wrote from his army in Spain to Lord Liverpool, then at the head of the British Government,--"In all times and places the sick-list of the army amounts to ten per cent of all.
10077{_}3074{_}"[17] He seemed to consider this the lowest attainable rate of sickness, and he hoped to be able to reduce that of his own army to it: this is more than five times as great as the rate of sickness among male civilians of the army-ages.
10077{_}3075{_}The sickness in Lord Wellington's army, at the moment of writing this despatch, was fifteen per cent., or seven and a half times as great as that at home.
10077{_}3076{_}In the same Peninsular War, there was of the sick in the French army a constant average of 136 per 1,000 in Spain, and 146 per 1,000 in Portugal.
10077{_}3077{_}Mr. Edmonds says, that, just before the Battle of Talavera, the French army consisted of 275,000 men, of whom 61,000, or 22.2 per cent., were sick.
10077{_}3078{_}[18] Lord Wellington wrote, Sept. 19, 1809, that the French army of 225,000 men had 30,000 to 40,000 sick, which is 13.3 to 17.7 per cent.
10077{_}3079{_}The French army in Portugal had at one time 64 per 1,000, and at another 235 per 1,000, and an average of 146 per 1,000, in the hospitals through the war.
10077{_}3080{_}The British army that fought the Battle of Waterloo, in 1815, had an average of 60,992 men, through the campaign of four months, June to September; of these, there was an average of 7,909, or 12.9 per cent., in the hospitals.
10077{_}3081{_}[19]  The British legion that went to Spain in 1836 consisted of 7,000 men.
10077{_}3082{_}Of these, 5,000, or 71 per cent., were admitted into the hospitals in three and a half months, and 1,223 died in six months.
10077{_}3083{_}This is equal to an annual rate of almost two and a half, 2.44, attacks for each man, and of 34.9 per cent.
10077{_}3084{_}mortality.
10077{_}3085{_}[20]  "Of 115,000 Russians who invaded Turkey in 1828 and 1829, only 10,000 or 15,000 ever repassed the Pruth.
10077{_}3086{_}The rest died there of intermittent fevers, dysenteries, and plague."
10077{_}3087{_}"From May, 1828, to February, 1829, 210,108 patients were admitted into the general and regimental hospitals."
10077{_}3088{_}"In October, 1828, 20,000 entered the general hospitals."
10077{_}3089{_}"The sickness was very fatal."
10077{_}3090{_}"More than a quarter of the fever-patients died."
10077{_}3091{_}"5,509 entered the hospitals, and of these, 3,959 died in August, 1829, and only 614 ultimately recovered."
10077{_}3092{_}"At Brailow the plague attacked 1,200 and destroyed 774."
10077{_}3093{_}"Dysentery was equally fatal."
10077{_}3094{_}"In the march across the Balkan, 1,000 men died of diarrhoea, fever, and scurvy."
10077{_}3095{_}"In Bulgaria, during July, 37,000 men were taken sick."
10077{_}3096{_}"At Adrianople a vast barrack was taken for a hospital, and in three days 1,616 patients were admitted.
10077{_}3097{_}On the first of September there were 3,666, and on the 15th, 4,646 patients in the house.
10077{_}3098{_}This was one-quarter of all the disposable force at that station."
10077{_}3099{_}"In October, 1,300 died of dysentery; and at the end of the month there were 4,700 in the hospitals."
10077{_}3100{_}"In the whole army the loss to the Russians in the year 1829 was at least 60,000 men.
10077{_}3101{_}"[21]  CRIMEAN WAR.
10077{_}3102{_}In 1854, twenty-five years after this fatal experience of the Russian army in Bulgaria, the British Government sent an army to the same province, where the men were exposed to the same diseases and suffered a similar depreciation of vital force in sickness and death.
10077{_}3103{_}For two years and more they struggled with these destructive influences in their own camps, in Bulgaria and the Crimea, with the usual result of such exposures in waste of life.
10077{_}3104{_}From April 10, 1854, to June 30, 1856, 82,901 British soldiers were sent to the Black Sea and its coasts; and through these twenty-six and two-thirds months the British army had an average of 34,559 men engaged in that "War in the East" with Russia.
10077{_}3105{_}From these there were furnished to the general and regimental, the stationary and movable hospitals 218,952 cases: 24,084, or 11 per cent, of these patients were wounded or injured in battle, and 194,868, or 89 per cent, suffered from the diseases of the camp.
10077{_}3106{_}This is equal to an annual average of two and a half attacks of sickness for each man.
10077{_}3107{_}The published reports give an analysis of only 162,123 of these cases of disease.
10077{_}3108{_}Of these, 110,673, or 68 per cent., were of the zymotic class,--fevers, dysenteries, scurvy, etc., which are generally supposed to be due to exposure and privation, and other causes which are subject to human control.
10077{_}3109{_}During the two years ending with March, 1856, 16,224 died of diseases, of which 14,476 were of the zymotic or preventable class, 2,755 were killed in battle, and 2,019 died of wounds and injuries received in battle.
10077{_}3110{_}The annual rate of mortality, from all diseases, was 23 per cent; from zymotic diseases, 21 per cent.
10077{_}3111{_}; from battle, 6.9 per cent.
10077{_}3112{_}The rate of sickness and mortality varied exceedingly in different months.
10077{_}3113{_}In April, May, and June, 1854, the deaths were at the annual rate of 8.7 per 1,000; in July, 159 per 1,000; in August and September, 310 per 1,000; in December, this rate again rose and reached 679 per 1,000; and in January, 1855, owing to the great exposures, hardships, and privations in the siege, and the very imperfect means of sustenance and protection, the mortality increased to the enormous rate of 1,142 per 1,000, so that, if it had continued unabated, it would have destroyed the whole army in ten and a half months.
10077{_}3114{_}[22]  AMERICAN ARMY, 1812 TO 1814.
10077{_}3115{_}We need not go abroad to find proofs of the waste of life in military camps.
10077{_}3116{_}Our own army, in the war with Great Britain in 1812-14, suffered, as the European armies have done, by sickness and death, far beyond men in civil occupations.
10077{_}3117{_}There are no comprehensive reports, published by the Government, of the sanitary condition and history of the army on the Northern frontier during that war.
10077{_}3118{_}But the partial and fragmentary statements of Dr. Mann, in his "Medical Sketches," and the occasional and apparently incidental allusions to the diseases and deaths by the commanding officers, in their letters and despatches to the Secretary of War, show that sickness was sometimes fearfully prevalent and fatal among our soldiers.
10077{_}3119{_}Dr. Mann says: "One regiment on the frontier, at one time, counted 900 strong, but was reduced, by a total want of a good police, to less than 200 fit for duty."
10077{_}3120{_}"At one period more than 340 were in the hospitals, and, in addition to this, a large number were reported sick in camp.
10077{_}3121{_}"[23] "The aggregate of the army at Fort George and its dependencies was about 5,000.
10077{_}3122{_}From an estimate of the number sick in the general and regimental hospitals, it was my persuasion that but little more than half of the army was capable of duty, at one period, during the summer months"[24] of 1813.
10077{_}3123{_}"During the month of August more than one-third of the soldiers were on the sick-reports.
10077{_}3124{_}"[25] Dr. Mann quotes Dr. Lovell, another army-surgeon, who says, in the autumn of 1813: "A morning report, now before me, gives 75 sick, out of a corps of 160.
10077{_}3125{_}The several regiments of the army, in their reports, exhibit a proportional number unfit for duty.
10077{_}3126{_}"[26] Dr. Mann states that "the troops at Burlington, Vt., in the winter of 1812-13, did not number over 1,600, and the deaths did not exceed 200, from the last of November to the last of February.
10077{_}3127{_}"[27] But Dr. Gallup says: "The whole number of deaths is said to be not less than 700 to 800 in four months," and "the number of soldiers stationed at this encampment [Burlington] was about 2,500 to 2,800.
10077{_}3128{_}"[28] According to Dr. Mann's statement, the mortality was at the annual rate of 50 per cent.
10077{_}3129{_}; and according to that of Dr. Gallup, it was at the rate of 75 to 96 per cent.
10077{_}3130{_}This is nearly equal to the severest mortality in the Crimea.
10077{_}3131{_}General William H. Harrison, writing to the Secretary of War from the borders of Lake Erie, Aug. 29, 1813, says: "You can form some estimate of the deadly effects of the immense body of stagnant water with which the vicinity of the lake abounds, from the state of the troops at Sandusky.
10077{_}3132{_}Upwards of 90 are this morning reported sick, out of about 220."
10077{_}3133{_}This is a rate of over 40 per cent.
10077{_}3134{_}"Those at Fort Meigs are not much better.
10077{_}3135{_}"[29]  General Wilkinson wrote from Fort George, Sept. 16, 1813: "We count, on paper, 4,600, and could show 3,400 combatants"; that is, 25 per cent, and more are sick.
10077{_}3136{_}"The enemy, from the best information we have, have about 3,000 on paper, of whom 1,400," or 46.6 per cent., "are sick.
10077{_}3137{_}"[30]  MEXICAN WAR.
10077{_}3138{_}There was a similar waste of life among our troops in the Mexican War.
10077{_}3139{_}There is no published record of the number of the sick, nor of their diseases.
10077{_}3140{_}But the letters of General Scott and General Taylor to the Secretary of War show that the loss of effective force in our army was at times very great by sickness in that war.
10077{_}3141{_}General Scott wrote:--     "_Puebla_, July 25, 1847.
10077{_}3142{_}"May 30, the number of sick here was 1,017, of effectives 5,820."
10077{_}3143{_}"Since the arrival of General Pillow, we have effectives (rank and    file) 8,061, sick 2,215, beside 87 officers under the latter    head.
10077{_}3144{_}"[31]  Again:--     "_Mexico_, Dec. 5,1847.
10077{_}3145{_}"The force at Chapultepec fit for duty is only about 6,000, rank and    file; the number of sick, exclusive of officers, being 2,041.
10077{_}3146{_}"[32]  According to these statements, the proportions of the sick were 17.4 to 27.4 and 24.7 per cent of all in these corps at the times specified.
10077{_}3147{_}General Taylor wrote:--     "_Camp near Monterey_, July 27,1847.
10077{_}3148{_}"Great sickness and mortality have prevailed among the volunteer    troops in front of Saltillo.
10077{_}3149{_}"[33]  August 10th, he said, that "nearly 23 per cent, of the force present was disabled by disease."
10077{_}3150{_}The official reports show only the number that died, but make no distinction as to causes of death, except to separate the deaths from wounds received in battle from those from other causes.
10077{_}3151{_}During that war, 100,454 men were sent to Mexico from the United States.
10077{_}3152{_}They were enlisted for various periods, but served, on an average, thirteen months and one day each, making a total of 109,104 years of military service rendered by our soldiers in that war.
10077{_}3153{_}The total loss of these men was 1,549 killed in battle or died of wounds, 10,986 died from diseases, making 12,535 deaths.
10077{_}3154{_}Besides these, 12,252 were discharged for disability.
10077{_}3155{_}The mortality from disease was almost equal to the annual rate of 11 per cent., which is about ten times as great as that of men in ordinary civil life at home.
10077{_}3156{_}SICKNESS IN THE PRESENT UNION ARMY.
10077{_}3157{_}There are not as yet, and for a long time there cannot be, any full Government reports of the amount and kind of sickness in the present army of the United States.
10077{_}3158{_}But the excellent reports of the inquiries of the Sanitary Commission give much important and trustworthy information in respect to these matters.
10077{_}3159{_}Most of the encampments of all the corps have been examined by their inspectors; and their returns show, that the average number sick, during the seven months ending with February last, was, among the troops who were recruited in New England 74.6, among those from the Middle States 56.6, and, during six months ending with January, among those from the Western States 104.3, in 1,000 men.
10077{_}3160{_}From an examination of 217 regiments, during two months ending the middle of February, the rate of sickness among the troops in the Eastern Sanitary Department was 74, in the Central Department, Western Virginia and Ohio, 90, and in the Western, 107, in 1,000 men.
10077{_}3161{_}The average of all these regiments was 90 in 1,000.
10077{_}3162{_}The highest rate in Eastern Virginia was 281 per 1,000, in the Fifth Vermont; and the lowest, 9, in the Seventh Massachusetts.
10077{_}3163{_}In the Central Department the highest was 260, in the Forty-First Ohio; and the lowest, 17, in the Sixth Ohio.
10077{_}3164{_}In the Western Department the highest was 340, in the Forty-Second Illinois; and the lowest, 15, in the Thirty-Sixth Illinois.
10077{_}3165{_}On the 22d of February, the number of men sick in each 1,000, in the several divisions of the Army of the Potomac, was ascertained to be,--   |  Keyes's,               |   30.3   | |  Sedgwick's,            |   32.0   | |  Hooker's,              |   43.7   | |  McCall's               |   44.4   | |  Banks's,               |   45.0   | |  Porter's,              |   46.4   | |  Blenker's,             |   47.7   | |  McDowell's,            |   48.2   | |  Heintzelman's          |   49.0   | |  Franklin's             |   54.1   | |  Dix's,                 |   71.8   | |  United States Regulars,|   76.0   | |  Sumner's,              |   77.5   | |  Smiths's,              |   81.6   | |  Casey's                |   87.6[34]  |   Probably there has been more sickness in all the armies, as they have gone farther southward and the warm season has advanced.
10077{_}3166{_}This would naturally be expected, and the fear is strengthened by the occasional reports in the newspapers.
10077{_}3167{_}Still, taking the trustworthy reports herein given, it is manifest that our Union army is one of the healthiest on record; and yet their rate of sickness is from three to five times as great as that of civilians of their own ages at home.
10077{_}3168{_}Unquestionably, this better condition of our men is due to the better intelligence of the age and of our people,--especially in respect to the dangers of the field and the necessity of proper provision on the part of the Government and of self-care on the part of the men,--to the wisdom, labors, and comprehensive watchfulness of the Sanitary Commission, and to the universal sympathy of the men and women of the land, who have given their souls, their hands, and their money to the work of lessening the discomforts and alleviating the sufferings of the Army of Freedom.
10077{_}3169{_}OTHER LIGHTER AND UNRECORDED SICKNESS.
10077{_}3170{_}The records and reports of the sickness in the army do not include all the depreciations and curtailments of life and strength among the soldiers, nor all the losses of effective force which the Government suffers through them, on account of disease and debility.
10077{_}3171{_}These records contain, at best, only such ailments as are of sufficient importance to come under the observation of the surgeon.
10077{_}3172{_}But there are manifold lighter physical disturbances, which, though they neither prostrate the patient, nor even cause him to go to the hospital, yet none the less certainly unfit him for labor and duty.
10077{_}3173{_}Of the regiment referred to by Dr. Mann, and already adduced in this article, in which 700 were unable to attend to duty, 340 were in the hospital under the surgeon's care, and 360 were ill in camp.
10077{_}3174{_}It is probable that a similar, though smaller, discrepancy often exists between the surgeon's records and the absentees from parades, guard-duty, etc.
10077{_}3175{_}It is improbable, and even impossible, that complete records and reports should always be made of all who are sick and unfit for duty, or even of all who come under the surgeon's care.
10077{_}3176{_}Sir John Hall, principal Medical Officer of the British army in the Crimea, says that there were "218,952 admissions into hospital.
10077{_}3177{_}"[35] "The general return, showing the primary admissions into the hospitals of the army in the East, from the 10th April, 1854, to the 30th June, 1856, gives only 162,123 cases of all kinds.
10077{_}3178{_}"[36] But another Government Report states the admissions to be 162,073.
10077{_}3179{_}[37] Miss Nightingale says, "There was, at first, no system of registration for general hospitals, for all were burdened with work beyond their strength.
10077{_}3180{_}"[38] Dr. Mann says, that, in the War of 1812, "no sick-records were found in the hospital at Burlington," one of the largest depositories of the sick then in the country.
10077{_}3181{_}"The hospital-records on the Niagara were under no order.
10077{_}3182{_}"[39] It could hardly have been otherwise.
10077{_}3183{_}The regimental hospitals then, as frequently must be the case in war, were merely extemporized shelters, not conveniences.
10077{_}3184{_}They were churches, houses, barns, shops, sheds, or any building that happened to be within reach, or huts, cabins, or tents suddenly created for the purpose.
10077{_}3185{_}In these all the surgeons' time, energy, and resources were expended in making their patients comfortable, in defending them from cold and storm, or from suffering in their crowded rooms or shanties.
10077{_}3186{_}They were obliged to devote all their strength to taking care of the present.
10077{_}3187{_}They could take little account of the past, and were often unable to make any record for the future.
10077{_}3188{_}They could not do this for those under their own immediate eye in the hospital; much less could they do it for those who remained in their tents, and needed little or no medical attention, but only rest.
10077{_}3189{_}Moreover, the exposures and labors of the campaign sometimes diminish the number and force of the surgeons as well as of the men, and reduce their strength at the very moment when the greatest demand is made for their exertions.
10077{_}3190{_}Dr. Mann says, "The sick in the hospital were between six and seven hundred, and there were only three surgeons present for duty."
10077{_}3191{_}"Of seven surgeons attached to the hospital department, one died, three were absent by reason of indisposition, and the other three were sick.
10077{_}3192{_}"[40] Fifty-four surgeons died in the Russian army in Turkey in the summer of 1828.
10077{_}3193{_}"At Brailow, the pestilence spared neither surgeons nor nurses.
10077{_}3194{_}"[41] Sir John Hall says, "The medical officers got sick, a great number went away, and we were embarrassed."
10077{_}3195{_}"Thirty per cent.
10077{_}3196{_}were sometimes sick and absent" from their posts in the Crimea.
10077{_}3197{_}[42] Seventy surgeons died in the French army in the same war.
10077{_}3198{_}It is not reasonable, then, to suppose that all or nearly all the cases of sickness, whether in hospital or in camp, can be recorded, especially at times when they are the most abundant.
10077{_}3199{_}Nor do the cases of sickness of every sort, grave and light, recorded and unrecorded, include all the depressions of vital energy and all the suspensions and loss of effective force in the army.
10077{_}3200{_}Whenever any general cause of depression weighs upon a body of men, as fatigue, cold, storm, privation of food, or malaria, it vitiates the power of all, in various degrees and with various results; the weak and susceptible are sickened, and all lose some force and are less able to labor and attend to duty.
10077{_}3201{_}No account is taken, none can be taken, of this discount of the general force of the army; yet it is none the less a loss of strength, and an impediment to the execution of the purposes of the Government.
10077{_}3202{_}INVALIDING.
10077{_}3203{_}The loss of force by death, by sickness in hospital and camp, and by temporary depression, is not all that the army is subject to.
10077{_}3204{_}Those who are laboring under consumption, asthma, epilepsy, insanity, and other incurable disorders, and those whose constitutions are broken, or withered and reduced below the standard of military requirement, are generally, and by some Governments always, discharged.
10077{_}3205{_}These pass back to the general community, where they finally die.
10077{_}3206{_}By this process the army is continually sifting out its worst lives, and at the same time it fills their places with healthy recruits.
10077{_}3207{_}It thus keeps up its average of health and diminishes its rate of mortality; but the sum and the rates of sickness and mortality in the community are both thereby increased.
10077{_}3208{_}During the Crimean War, 17.34 per cent, were invalided and sent home from the British army, and 21 per cent, from the French army, as unable to do military service.
10077{_}3209{_}By this means, 11,994[43] British and 65,069[44] French soldiers were lost to their Governments.
10077{_}3210{_}The army of the United States, in the Mexican War, discharged and sent home 12,252 men, or 12 per cent, of the entire number engaged in that war, on account of disability.
10077{_}3211{_}The causes of this exhaustion of personal force are manifold and various, and so generally present that the number and proportion of those who are thus hopelessly reduced below the degree of efficient military usefulness, in the British army, has been determined by observation, and the Government calculates the rate of the loss which will happen in this way, at any period of service.
10077{_}3212{_}Out of 10,000 men enlisted in their twenty-first year, 718 will be invalided during the first quinquennial period, or before they pass their twenty-fifth year, 539 in the second, 673 in the third, and 854 in the fourth,--making 2,784, or more than one-quarter of the whole, discharged for disability or chronic ailment, before they complete their twenty years of military service and their forty years of life.
10077{_}3213{_}It is further to be considered, that, during these twenty years, the numbers are diminishing by death, and thus the ratio of enfeebled and invalided is increased.
10077{_}3214{_}Out of 10,000 soldiers who survive and remain in the army in each successive quinquennial period, 768 will be invalided in the first, 680 in the second, 1,023 in the third, and 1,674 in the fourth.
10077{_}3215{_}In the first year the ratio is 181, in the fifth 129, in the tenth 165, in the fifteenth 276, and in the twentieth 411, among 10,000 surviving and remaining.
10077{_}3216{_}The depressing and exhaustive force of military life on the soldiers is gradually accumulative, or the power of resistance gradually wastes, from the beginning to the end of service.
10077{_}3217{_}There is an apparent exception to this law in the fact, that, in the British army, the ratio of those who were invalided was 181 in 10,000, but diminished, in the second, third, and fourth years, to 129 in the fifth and sixth, then again rose, through all the succeeding years, to 411 in the twentieth.
10077{_}3218{_}The experience of the British army, in this respect, is corroborated by that of ours in the Mexican War.
10077{_}3219{_}From the old standing army 502, from the additional force recently enlisted 548, and from the volunteers 1,178, in 10,000 of each, were discharged on account of disability.
10077{_}3220{_}Some part of this great difference between the regulars and volunteers is doubtless due to the well-known fact, that the latter were originally enlisted, in part at least, for domestic trainings, and not for the actual service of war, and therefore were examined with less scrutiny, and included more of the weaker constitutions.
10077{_}3221{_}The Sanitary Commission, after inspecting two hundred and seventeen regiments of the present army of the United States, and comparing the several corps with each other in respect of health, came to a similar conclusion.
10077{_}3222{_}They found that the twenty-four regiments which had the least sickness had been in service one hundred and forty days on an average, and the twenty-four regiments which had the most sickness had been in the field only one hundred and eleven days.
10077{_}3223{_}The Actuary adds, in explanation,--"The difference between the sickness of the older and newer regiments is probably attributable, in part, to the constant weeding out of the sickly by discharges from the service.
10077{_}3224{_}The fact is notorious, that medical inspection of recruits, on enlistment, has been, as a rule, most imperfectly executed; and the city of Washington is constantly thronged with invalids awaiting their discharge-papers, who at the time of their enlistment were physically unfit for service.
10077{_}3225{_}"[45] In addition to this, it must be remembered, that, although all recruits are apparently perfect in form and free from disease when they enter the army, yet there may be differences in constitutional force, which cannot be detected by the most careful examiners.
10077{_}3226{_}Some have more and some have less power of endurance.
10077{_}3227{_}But the military burden and the work of war are arranged and determined for the strongest, and, of course, break down the weak, who retire in disability or sink in death.
10077{_}3228{_}GENERAL VITAL DEPRESSION  Two causes of depression operate, to a considerable degree in peace and to a very great degree in war, on the soldier, and reduce and sicken him more than the civilian.
10077{_}3229{_}His vital force is not so well sustained by never-failing supplies of nutritious and digestible food and regular nightly sleep, and his powers are more exhausted in hardships and exposures, in excessive labors and want of due rest and protection against cold and heat, storms and rains.
10077{_}3230{_}Consequently the army suffers mostly from diseases of depression,--those of the typhoid, adynamic, and scorbutic types.
10077{_}3231{_}McGrigor says, that, in the British army in the Peninsula, of 176,007 cases treated and recorded by the surgeons, 68,894 were fevers, 23,203 diseases of the bowels, 12,167 ulcers, and 4,027 diseases of the lungs.
10077{_}3232{_}[46] In the British hospitals in the Crimean War, 39 per cent.
10077{_}3233{_}were cholera, dysentery, and diarrhoea, 19 per cent.
10077{_}3234{_}fevers, 1.2 per cent.
10077{_}3235{_}scurvy, 8 per cent.
10077{_}3236{_}diseases of the lungs, 8 per cent.
10077{_}3237{_}diseases of the skin, 3.3 per cent.
10077{_}3238{_}rheumatism, 2.5 per cent.
10077{_}3239{_}diseases of the brain and nervous system, 1.4 per cent.
10077{_}3240{_}frost-bite or mortification produced by low vitality and chills, 13, or one in 12,000, had sunstroke, 257 had the itch, and 68 per cent.
10077{_}3241{_}of all were of the zymotic class,[47] which are considered as principally due to privation, exposure, and personal neglect.
10077{_}3242{_}The deaths from these classes of causes were in a somewhat similar proportion to the mortality from all stated causes,--being 58 per cent.
10077{_}3243{_}from cholera, dysentery, and diarrhoea, and 1 per cent.
10077{_}3244{_}from all other disorders of the digestive organs, 19 per cent.
10077{_}3245{_}from fevers, 3.6 per cent.
10077{_}3246{_}from diseases of the lungs, 1.3 per cent.
10077{_}3247{_}from rheumatism, 1.3 per cent.
10077{_}3248{_}from diseases of the brain and nervous system, and 79 per cent.
10077{_}3249{_}from those of the zymotic class.
10077{_}3250{_}The same classes of disease, with a much larger proportion of typhoid, pneumonia, prostrated and destroyed many in the American army in the War of 1812.
10077{_}3251{_}In paper No.
10077{_}3252{_}40, p. 54, of the Sanitary Commission, is a report of the diseases that occurred in forty-nine regiments, while under inspection about forty days each, between July and October, 1861.
10077{_}3253{_}27,526 cases were reported; of these 67 per cent.
10077{_}3254{_}were zymotic, 41 per cent.
10077{_}3255{_}diseases of the digestive organs, 22 per cent.
10077{_}3256{_}fevers 7 per cent.
10077{_}3257{_}diseases of the lungs, 5 per cent.
10077{_}3258{_}diseases of the brain.
10077{_}3259{_}Among males of the army-ages the proportions of deaths from these classes of causes to those from all causes were, in Massachusetts, in 1859, zymotic 15 per cent., diseases of digestive organs 3.6 per cent., of lungs 50 per cent., fevers 9 per cent., diseases of brain 4.6 per cent[48].
10077{_}3260{_}According to the mortality-statistics of the seventh census of the United States, of the males between the ages of twenty and fifty, in Maryland, Virginia, North Carolina, South Carolina, and Georgia, whose deaths in the year ending June 1st, 1850, and their causes, were ascertained and reported by the marshals, 34.3 per cent.
10077{_}3261{_}died of zymotic diseases, 8 per cent.
10077{_}3262{_}of all the diseases of the digestive organs, 30.8 per cent.
10077{_}3263{_}of diseases of the respiratory organs, 24.4 per cent.
10077{_}3264{_}of fevers, and 5.7 per cent.
10077{_}3265{_}of disorders of the brain and nervous system.
10077{_}3266{_}In England and Wales, in 1858, these proportions were, zymotic 14 per cent., fevers 8 per cent., diseases of digestive organs 7.9 per cent., of lungs 8 per cent., and of the brain 7 per cent[49].
10077{_}3267{_}If, however, we analyze the returns of mortality in civil life, and distinguish those of the poor and neglected dwellers in the crowded and filthy lanes and alleys of cities, whose animal forces are not well developed, or are reduced by insufficient and uncertain nutrition, by poor food or bad cookery, by foul air within and stenchy atmosphere without, by imperfect protection of house and clothing, we shall find the same diseases there as in the army.
10077{_}3268{_}Wherever the vital forces are depressed, there these diseases of low vitality happen most frequently and are most fatal.
10077{_}3269{_}Volumes of other facts and statements might be quoted to show that military service is exhaustive of vital force more than the pursuits of civil life.
10077{_}3270{_}It is so even in time of peace, and it is remarkably so in time of war.
10077{_}3271{_}Comparing the English statements of the mortality in the army with the calculations of the expectation of life in the general community, the difference is at once manifest.
10077{_}3272{_}Of 10,000 men at the age of twenty, there will die before they complete their fortieth year,--   British army in time of peace,         3,058 England and Wales, English Life-Table, 1,853 According to tables of Amicable and Equitable Life-Insurance Companies,    1,972 New England and New York, according to the tables of the New-England Mutual Life-Insurance Company,         1,721   DANGERS IN LAND-BATTLES.
10077{_}3273{_}This large amount of disease and mortality in the army arises not from the battle-field, but belongs to the camp, the tent, the barrack, the cantonment; and it is as certain, though not so great, in time of peace, when no harm is inflicted by the instruments of destruction, as in time of war.
10077{_}3274{_}The battle, which is the world's terror, is comparatively harmless.
10077{_}3275{_}The official histories of the deadly struggles of armies show that they are not so wasteful of life as is generally supposed.
10077{_}3276{_}Mr. William Barwick Hodge examined the records and despatches in the War-Office in London, and from these and other sources prepared an exceedingly valuable and instructive paper on "The Mortality arising from Military Operations," which was read before the London Statistical Society, and printed in the nineteenth volume of the Society's journal.
10077{_}3277{_}Some of the tables will be as interesting to Americans as to Englishmen.
10077{_}3278{_}On the following page is a tabular view, taken from this work, of the casualties in nineteen battles fought by the British armies with those of other nations.

10077{_}3734{_}.
10077{_}3735{_}3,787                                ------                                86,864     98  Of those who were engaged in these nineteen battles one in 51.6, or 1.93 per cent., were killed.
10077{_}3736{_}The deaths in consequence of the battles, including both those who died of wounds and those that died among the missing, were one in 30, or 3.3 per cent.
10077{_}3737{_}of all who were in the fight.
10077{_}3738{_}It is worth noticing here, that the British loss in the Battle of New Orleans was larger than in any other battle here adduced, except in that of Albuera, in Spain, with the French, in 1811.
10077{_}3739{_}In the British army, from 1793 to 1815, including twenty-one years of war, and excluding 1802, the year of peace, the number of officers varied from 3,576 in the first year to 13,248 in 1813, and the men varied from 74,500 in 1793 to 276,000 in 1813, making an annual average of 9,078 officers and 189,200 men, and equal to 199,727 officers and 4,168,500 men serving one year.
10077{_}3740{_}During these twenty-one years of war, among the officers 920 were killed and 4,685 were wounded, and among the men 15,392 were killed and 65,393 were wounded.
10077{_}3741{_}This is an annual average of deaths from battle of 460 officers and 369 men, and of wounded 2,340 officers and 1,580 men, among 100,000 of each class.
10077{_}3742{_}Of the officers less than half of one per cent., or 1 in 217, were killed, and a little more than two per cent., or 1 in 42, were wounded; and among the men a little more than a third of one per cent., 1 in 271, were killed, and one and a half per cent., 1 in 63, wounded, in each year.
10077{_}3743{_}The comparative danger to the two is, of death, 46 officers to 37 men, and of wounds, 234 officers to 158 men.
10077{_}3744{_}A larger proportion of the officers than of the soldiers were killed and wounded; yet a larger proportion of the wounded officers recovered.
10077{_}3745{_}This is attributed to the fact that the officers were injured by rifle-balls, being picked out by the marksmen, while the soldiers were injured by cannon- and musket-balls and shells, which inflict more deadly injuries.
10077{_}3746{_}DANGERS IN NAVAL BATTLES.
10077{_}3747{_}It may not be out of place here to show the dangers of naval warfare, which are discussed at length by Mr. Hodge, in a very elaborate paper in the eighteenth volume of the Statistical Society's journal.
10077{_}3748{_}From one of his tables, containing a condensed statistical history of the English navy, through the wars with France, 1792-1815, the following facts are gathered.
10077{_}3749{_}During those wars, the British Parliament, in its several annual grants, voted 2,527,390 men for the navy.
10077{_}3750{_}But the number actually in the service is estimated not to have exceeded 2,424,000 in all, or a constant average force of 110,180 men.
10077{_}3751{_}Within this time these men fought five hundred and seventy-six naval battles, and they were exposed to storms, to shipwreck, and to fire, in every sea.
10077{_}3752{_}In all these exposures, the records show that the loss of life was less than was suffered by the soldiers on the land.

10077{_}3863{_}This is condensed and quoted on the preceding page.
10077{_}3864{_}His third table includes thirty-five actions with single ships on each side, between the years 1793 and 1815.
10077{_}3865{_}8,542 men were engaged, and 483, or 56.5 per 1,000, were killed, and 1,230, or 144 per 1,000, wounded.
10077{_}3866{_}Twenty-six of these actions were with French ships, which are here omitted, and nine with American ships, which are shown in the second table on the preceding page.

10077{_}3875{_}The dangers in fleet-battles were about half as great as those in land-battles, and these were but little more than half as great as those in fights with single ships.
10077{_}3876{_}COMPARATIVE DANGER OF CAMP AND BATTLE-FIELD.
10077{_}3877{_}These records of land-battles show that the dangers from that cause are not very great; probably they are less than the world imagines; certainly they are much less than those of the camp.
10077{_}3878{_}Of the 176,007 admitted into the regimental hospitals during the Peninsular War, only 20,886 were from wounds, the rest from diseases; fourteen-fifteenths of the burden on the hospitals in that war, through forty-two months, were diseased patients, and only one-fifteenth were wounded.
10077{_}3879{_}In the Crimean War, 11.2 per cent.
10077{_}3880{_}in the hospitals suffered from injuries in battle, and 88.8 per cent.
10077{_}3881{_}from other causes.
10077{_}3882{_}10 per cent.
10077{_}3883{_}of the French patients in the same war were wounded, and 90 per cent.
10077{_}3884{_}had fevers, etc.
10077{_}3885{_}In the autumn of 1814, there were 815 patients in the great military hospital at Burlington, Vermont.
10077{_}3886{_}Of these 50 were wounded, and the rest had the diseases of the camp.
10077{_}3887{_}In the Crimean War, 16,296 died from disease, and 4,774 from injuries received in battle.
10077{_}3888{_}In the Peninsular War, 25,304 died of disease, and 9,450 from wounds.
10077{_}3889{_}During eighteen years, 1840 to 1857, 19,504 were discharged from the home, and 21,325 from the foreign stations of the British army.
10077{_}3890{_}Of these, 541, or 2.7 per cent.
10077{_}3891{_}of those at home, and 3,708, or 17.3 per cent.
10077{_}3892{_}abroad, were on account of wounds and fractures, and the others on account of disease, debility, and exhaustion.
10077{_}3893{_}NATIONS DO NOT LEARN FROM EXPERIENCE TO PREPARE FOR ARMY-SICKNESS.
10077{_}3894{_}Nations, when they go to war, prepare to inflict injury and death on their opponents, and make up their minds to receive the same in return; but they seem neither to look nor to prepare for sickness and death in their camps.
10077{_}3895{_}And when these come upon their armies, they seem either to shut their eyes to the facts, or submit to the loss as to a disturbance in Nature, a storm, a drought, or an earthquake, which they can neither prevent nor provide for, and for which they feel no responsibility, but only hope that it will not happen again.
10077{_}3896{_}Nevertheless, this waste of life has followed every army which has been made to violate the laws of health, in privations, exposures, and hardships, and whose internal history is known.
10077{_}3897{_}The experience of such disastrous campaigns ought to induce Governments to inquire into the causes of the suffering and loss, and to learn whether they are not engaged in a struggle against Nature, in which they must certainly fail, and endeavoring to make the human body bear burdens and labors which are beyond its strength.
10077{_}3898{_}But Governments are slow to learn, especially sanitary lessons.
10077{_}3899{_}The British army suffered and died in great numbers at Walcheren and South Beveland, in the middle of the last century.
10077{_}3900{_}Pringle described the sad condition of those troops, and warned his nation against a similar exposure; yet, sixty years later, the Ministry sent another army to the same place, to sink under the malarious influences and diseases in the same way.
10077{_}3901{_}The English troops at Jamaica were stationed in the low grounds, where, "for many generations," "the average annual mortality was 13 per cent."
10077{_}3902{_}"A recommendation for their removal from the plains to the mountains was made so far back as 1791.
10077{_}3903{_}Numerous reports were sent to the Government, advising that a higher situation should be selected"; but it was not until 1837, after nearly half a century of experience and warning, that the Ministry opened their eyes to this cost of life and money in excessive sickness and mortality, and then removed the garrison to Maroontown, where the death-rate fell to 2 per cent., or less than one-sixth of what it had been[50].
10077{_}3904{_}The American army, in the war with Great Britain fifty years ago, suffered from the want of proper provision for their necessities and comfort, from exposures and hardships, so that sometimes half its force was unavailable; yet, at the present moment, a monstrous army is collected and sent to the field, under the same regulations, and with the same idea of man's indefinite power of endurance, and the responsibility and superintendence of their health is left, in large measure, to an accidental and outside body of men, the Sanitary Commission, which, although an institution of great heart and energy, and supported by the sympathies and cooperation of the whole people, is yet doing a work that ought to be done by the Government, and carrying out a plan of operations that should be inseparably associated with the original creation of the army and the whole management of the war.
10077{_}3905{_}CRIMEAN WAR.
10077{_}3906{_}The lesson which the experience of the Russian army of 1828 and 1829 taught the world of the mortal dangers of Bulgaria was lost on the British Government, which sent its own troops there in 1854, to be exposed to, and wither before, the same destructive influences.
10077{_}3907{_}But at length sickness prevailed to such an extent, and death made such havoc, in the army in the East, that England's great sympathies were roused, and the Ministers' attention was drawn to the irresistible fact, that the strongest of Britain's soldiers were passing rapidly from the camp to the hospital, and from the hospital to the grave.
10077{_}3908{_}Then a doubt occurred to the minds of the men in power, whether all was right in the Crimea, and whether something might not be done for the sanitary salvation of the army.
10077{_}3909{_}They sent a commission, consisting of Dr. John Sutherland, one of the ablest sanitarians of the kingdom, Dr. Hector Gavin, and Robert Rawlinson, civil engineer, to the Black Sea, to inquire into the state of things there, to search out the causes of the sufferings of the army, and see if there might not be a remedy found and applied.
10077{_}3910{_}At the same time, Miss Nightingale and a large corps of assistants, attendants, and nurses, women of station and culture and women of hire, went to that terrible scene of misery and death, to aid in any measures that might be devised to alleviate the condition of the men.
10077{_}3911{_}Great abuses and negligence were found; and the causes of disease were manifest, manifold, and needless.
10077{_}3912{_}But a reform was at once instituted; great changes were made in the general management of the camp and hospitals and in the condition of the soldiers.
10077{_}3913{_}Disease began to diminish, the progress of mortality was arrested, and in the course of a few months the rate of death was as low as among men of the same ages at home.
10077{_}3914{_}This commission made a full report, when they returned, and described the state of things they found in the Crimea and on the shores of the Black Sea,--the camps, barracks, huts, tents, food, manner of life, and general sanitary condition of the troops, their terrible sufferings, and the means and ways of caring for the sick, the measures of reform which they had proposed and carried out, and their effects on the health of the men.
10077{_}3915{_}This report was published by the Government.
10077{_}3916{_}Besides this commission, the Government sent Dr. Lyons, a surgeon and pathologist of great learning and acumen, to investigate the pathology or morbid condition of the army.
10077{_}3917{_}According to his instructions, he spent four months in the Crimea and at the great hospitals on the Bosphorus.
10077{_}3918{_}He examined and traced the course of disease and disturbance in the sick and wounded.
10077{_}3919{_}He made very many thorough examinations after death, in order to determine the effects of vitiating influences upon the organization, and the condition of the textures and organs of the body in connection with the several kinds of disorders.
10077{_}3920{_}Dr. Lyons's extremely instructive report was published by national authority as one of the Parliamentary folio volumes.
10077{_}3921{_}After the war was over, Dr. W. Hanbury and Staff-Surgeon Matthew, under the direction of the Secretary of War, gathered, analyzed, and prepared the records of all the surgeons of the several corps of the Crimean army.
10077{_}3922{_}To these they added a long and valuable treatise on the nature and character of the diseases, and their connection with the condition and habits of the men.
10077{_}3923{_}These are published in two very thick folio volumes, and give a minute and almost daily history of the life, labors, exposures, privations, sufferings, sickness, and mortality of each regiment.
10077{_}3924{_}These two works, of Dr. Lyons and Drs.
10077{_}3925{_}Hanbury and Matthew, show the inseparable connection between the manner of living and the health, and demonstrate that the severe life of war, with its diminished creation of vital force, by imperfect and uncertain nutrition and excessive expenditure in exposures and labors, necessarily breaks down the constitution.
10077{_}3926{_}It subjects the body to more abundant disorders, and especially to those of the depressive, adynamic type, which, from the want of the usual recuperative power, are more fatal than the diseases of civil life.
10077{_}3927{_}These works may be considered generic as well as specific.
10077{_}3928{_}They apply to and describe the sanitary condition and the pathological history of all armies engaged in hard and severe campaigns, as well as those of the Crimea.
10077{_}3929{_}They should, therefore, be read by every Government that engages in or is forced into any war.
10077{_}3930{_}They should be distributed to and thoroughly understood by every commander who directs the army, and every surgeon who superintends the sanitary condition of, and manages the sickness among, the men; and happy will it be for those soldiers whose military and sanitary directors avail themselves of the instructions contained in these volumes.
10077{_}3931{_}There are several other works on the Crimean War, by surgeons and other officers, written mainly to give a knowledge of the general facts of those campaigns, but all incidentally corroborating and explaining the statements in the Government Reports, in respect to the health and sufferings of the British and French armies.
10077{_}3932{_}In this view, Dr. Bryce's book, "England and France before Sebastopol," and M. Baudens's and M. Scrive's medical works in French, are worthy of great attention and confidence.
10077{_}3933{_}The most important and valuable work, in this connection, is the Report of the British Commission appointed in May, 1854, "to inquire into the regulations affecting the sanitary condition of the British army, the organization of the military hospitals, and the treatment of the sick and wounded."
10077{_}3934{_}This commission included some of the ablest and most learned physicians and surgeons in the civil and military service, some of the most accomplished statisticians, sanitarians, army-officers, and statesmen in the United Kingdom.
10077{_}3935{_}They were authorized to inquire into the habits and duties, the moral and sanitary condition of the army, the amount and kinds of sickness, the causes and frequency of death, and the means of improvement.
10077{_}3936{_}This commission sat for a long time in London.
10077{_}3937{_}They called before them fifty-three witnesses, among whom were Sir Benjamin Brodie, the leading surgeon of England, Dr. Andrew Smith, Director-General of the Medical Department of the Army, Thomas Alexander, Inspector-General of Hospitals, Major-General Airey, Quartermaster-General, Dr. John Sutherland, late Crimean Commissioner, and one of the leading authorities of Great Britain in all sanitary matters, Dr. William Fair, the chief and master-spirit of the Registry-Office, and the highest authority in vital statistics, Colonel Sir Alexander Tulloch, author of the elaborate and valuable reports on the mortality in the British army, Francis G. P. Neison, author of "Contributions to Vital Statistics," Miss Nightingale, and others, surgeons, officers, purveyors, engineers, soldiers, and medical and sanitary scholars.
10077{_}3938{_}The commission put forth 10,070 interrogatories relating to everything connected with the army, the persons and the _materiel_, to officers, surgeons, physicians, health-officers, soldiers, nurses, cooks, clothing, food, cooking, barracks, tents, huts, hospitals, duties, labors, exposures, and privations, and their effects on health and life, in every climate, wherever British troops are stationed or serve, at home and abroad.
10077{_}3939{_}The same inquiry was extended to the armies of other nations, French, Turkish, Russian, etc.
10077{_}3940{_}To these questions the witnesses returned answers, and statements of facts and opinions, all carefully prepared, and some of great length, and elaborate calculations in respect to the whole military and sanitary science and practice of the age.
10077{_}3941{_}A large part of the inquiry was directed to the Crimean army, whose condition had been, and was then, a matter of the most intense interest.
10077{_}3942{_}Many of the witnesses had, in various ways, been connected with that war: they were familiar with its history, and their answers revealed much that had not before been known.
10077{_}3943{_}The result of all this investigation is published in a folio volume of 607 pages, filled with facts and principles, the lamentable history of the past, painful descriptions of the present, and wise suggestions for the future management of the army; and the whole is worthy of the careful attention of all who, as projectors, leaders, or followers, have anything to do with the active operations of war.
10077{_}3944{_}The Crimean War has this remarkable interest, not that the suffering of the troops and their depreciation in effective power were greater than in many other wars, but that these happened in an age when the intelligence and philanthropy, and even the policy of the nation, demanded to know whether the vital depression and the loss of martial strength were as great as rumor reported, whether these were the necessary condition of war, and whether anything could be done to lessen them.
10077{_}3945{_}By the investigations and reports of commissions, officers, and others, the internal history of this war is more completely revealed and better known than that of any other on record.
10077{_}3946{_}It is placed on a hill, in the sight of all nations and governments, for their observation and warning, to be faithful to the laws of health in providing for, and in the use of, their armies, if they would obtain the most efficient service from them.
10077{_}3947{_}WANT OF SANITARY PREPARATIONS FOR WAR.
10077{_}3948{_}There are, and have been, faults--grievous, destructive, and costly faults--in all connected with armies, from the Governments at the head, down through all grades of officers, to the men in the ranks: they are faults of theory and faults of practice,--of plan in those who direct, and of self-management in those whose whole duty is to obey.
10077{_}3949{_}The root of this is the failure to fully understand and count the cost, and to prepare to meet it as men generally do in the management of their common affairs.
10077{_}3950{_}In civil life, when prudent men intend to effect any purpose by the aid of motive power, whether of water, steam, horse, or other kind, they carefully consider the means of generating that power, and the best and safest ways of applying and expending it.
10077{_}3951{_}They include this in their plans, and make provision accordingly.
10077{_}3952{_}Precisely determining the extent of the purpose they design to effect, and the amount of force that is and will be needed, they make their arrangements to provide or generate and maintain so much as long as they intend to do the work.
10077{_}3953{_}During the whole process, they carefully guard and treasure it up and allow none to be wasted or applied to any other than the appointed purpose.
10077{_}3954{_}But in the use and management of the vital machines, the human bodies, by which the purposes of war are to be accomplished, nations are less wise.
10077{_}3955{_}There are few, perhaps no records of any Government, which, in creating, maintaining and operating with an army, has, at and during the same time, created and established the never-failing means of keeping the machinery of war in the best working order, by sustaining the health and force of the men in unfailing fulness.
10077{_}3956{_}War is carried on by a partnership between the Government and soldiers, to which the Government contributes money and directing skill, and assumes the responsibility of management, and the soldiers contribute their vital force.
10077{_}3957{_}In the operation of this joint concern, both the money of the nation and the lives of the men are put at risk.
10077{_}3958{_}Although, by the terms of the contract, the Government is presumed to expend its money and the soldiers' vital force to the extent that may be necessary to effect the objects of the association, it has no right to do this for any other purpose or on any other condition.
